Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
8 min
Naskh by Qiyas: Why Analogy Cannot Abrogate Revelation
Commentary on the invalidity of abrogation by opinion and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects abrogation by qiyas and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Qiyas can specify but cannot abrogate – abrogation requires certain knowledge that human reason cannot provide.

In this passage from Noor al-Anwar, the commentator tackles a fundamental usul dispute: can abrogation (naskh) occur through analogical reasoning (qiyas) or through human opinion (ra'y)? The Hanafi position is a firm 'no', supported by a hadith of 'Ali that illustrates how revealed practice overrides rational preference. Some Shafi'i scholars disagreed, arguing that since naskh is a form of clarification (bayan) like specification (takhsis), what is permissible for takhsis should be permissible for naskh. This lesson examines the Hanafi refutation of that analogy and the epistemological basis for limiting naskh to textual sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The central question: can a ruling from the Quran or Sunnah be abrogated by qiyas (analogy) or by ra'y (personal opinion)?
This is distinct from using qiyas to derive new rulings; here the issue is overriding an established revealed text.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school holds that naskh cannot occur by qiyas. The proof is the hadith of 'Ali: the Prophet wiped over the outside of the khuff, which is contrary to what rational opinion would suggest, showing that revelation takes precedence over opinion. Thus, if religion were by opinion, the inside would be more deserving.
This hadith is used as a general principle that religious rulings are based on revelation, not reason.
3
State the opposing Shafi'i position and their proof
Ibn Surayj and al-Anmati, among the Shafi'is, permitted naskh by ra'y and by qiyas derived from the text. Their proof: naskh is a type of bayan (clarification), just like takhsis (specification). Since takhsis can be done by reason (e.g., 'aql can specify a general text based on context), naskh should also be possible by reason.
Al-Anmati specifically argued that a qiyas derived from the Quran can abrogate part of the Quran, effectively making it a form of naskh of Book by Book.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between takhsis and naskh: takhsis is clarification (bayan) of what the text already meant, while naskh is removal (raf') and nullification (ibtaal) of the ruling. Reason can clarify but cannot legislate removal. Furthermore, the effective cause (illa) in a qiyas is never certain enough to abrogate a definitive text; thus, the analogy fails.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on certainty: naskh requires qat'i evidence, while qiyas provides only zann.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if naskh by qiyas were allowed, revealed texts could be overridden by juristic reasoning, weakening the authority of the Quran and Sunnah. The Hanafi position preserves the primacy of revelation and limits legislative authority to texts of equal or superior certainty.
This also affects how later generations treat rulings: without the prohibition of naskh by qiyas, no ruling from the primary sources would be secure.

Section on the types of Bayan. Statement on abrogation. Religion [would be] by opinion, then the inside of the khuff would be more deserving of wiping than its outside, but I saw the Messenger of Allah wiping over the outside of the khuff, not its inside. Likewise, consensus is in the meaning of the Book and Sunnah. As for the fact that analogy is not an abrogator of analogy, it is because if two analogies conflict at one time, the mujtahid acts on whichever he wills by the testimony of his heart; and if they are in two times, he acts on the latter analogy among those referred to, but that is not called abrogation in terminology. Ibn Surayj, a follower of al-Shafi‘i, permitted abrogation of the Book and Sunnah by opinion; and al-Anmati among them permitted abrogation of the Book by an analogy derived from it. Likewise, consensus according to the majority is not valid as an abrogator of any evidence because it is an expression of the gathering of opinions. If analogy is not an abrogator of the Book and Sunnah, then it is also not an abrogator of consensus. (Al-Qamar) 'The mujtahid acts on the latter etc.' not because the latter analogy indicates the end of the ruling established by the first analogy, for opinion has no entry into knowing the end of goodness or badness; rather, because he knows at that time that the first analogy was not correct, so he leaves it and does not act on it. (Al-Qamar) 'Abrogation etc.' for lack of truth of the definition of abrogation as mentioned earlier. (Al-Qamar) 'He permits etc.' because abrogation is a clarification like specification; so what is permissible for specification is also permissible for abrogation. We say: The analogy of abrogation to specification has a difference; for the indication of reason can be specifying but cannot be abrogating, so how can they be equal? For specification is clarification, while abrogation is removal and nullification. (Al-Qamar) 'Al-Anmati among them etc.' i.e., Abu al-Qasim al-Anmati from the companions of al-Shafi‘i said: Every analogy derived from the Quran permits abrogation of the Quran by it; likewise every analogy derived from the Sunnah permits abrogation of the Sunnah by it. This is, in reality, abrogation of the Book by the Book and abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah. However, the description by which the branch is referred back to the original text mentioned in the Book and Sunnah is not certain as being the meaning in the ruling established in the text. If that meaning were certain, being specified in the text, then abrogation by it would be permissible just like the text. (Al-Qamar) 'Al-Anmati' in some manuscripts here has a different expression. (Al-Muhashi) 'According to the majority' i.e., it is not an abrogator of any evidence according to the majority, nor can it be abrogated; because the agreement of all on a ruling without temporal limitation indicates that it is good or evil by itself, not susceptible to falling. (Al-Sunbuli) 'Of any evidence' i.e., the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy. (Al-Qamar) 'Because it' i.e., consensus is an expression of gathering of opinions; this is loose; because consensus is transitive, and gathering is intransitive; how is the attribution and interpretation valid? Unless it is taken as an interpretation from the perspective of what results from consensus. (Al-Qamar) Abu Dawud narrated no. 162, chapter on how to wipe, from ‘Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: If religion were by opinion, then the bottom of the khuff would be more deserving of wiping than its top; but I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wiping over the top of his khuff. (Page 598) ... Statement on abrogation. Section on the types of Bayan. And the end of goodness is not known by opinion. And Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is permissible to abrogate consensus by consensus. Perhaps he meant that consensus is conceivable to be for a benefit, then that benefit changes, so a new consensus arises abrogating the first. According to some Mu‘tazila, it is permissible to abrogate the Book by consensus, because 'those whose hearts are reconciled' (mu’allafatu qulubuhum) (may Allah be pleased with them) are mentioned in the Book, and their share of charity fell by the consensus that was formed in the time of Abu Bakr. We said: That was of the type of ending of the ruling due to the ending of its cause. And it was said: That was abrogated by a hadith he narrated during the caliphate of Abu Bakr, and they agreed on its authenticity, but the hadith was forgotten from hearts. (i.e., when opinions gather) And it is not known by opinion, so the ummah cannot know the duration of the ruling. And abrogation is a clarification of the duration of the ruling and its being good up to that time; so how can consensus be an abrogator? (Al-Qamar) 'And Fakhr al-Islam said' i.e., al-Bazdawi in the chapter on consensus. (Al-Qamar) 'Perhaps he meant etc.' Know that Fakhr al-Islam said in the chapter on abrogation that abrogation by consensus does not occur, and he said in the chapter on consensus that abrogation of consensus by consensus is permissible; so there is contradiction between his statements. The commentator (al-Sharh) deflects this with this saying. Its summary: That consensus cannot be formed contrary to the Book and Sunnah, so it cannot abrogate them; that is the meaning of what he said in the chapter on abrogation. And what he said in the chapter on consensus perhaps he meant etc. (Al-Qamar) 'Then a new consensus arises abrogating etc.' Because the goodness of the previous ruling was due to that benefit; and when the benefit changed, it was known that it is not good, so another consensus arose by Allah's grace abrogating the first. (Al-Qamar) 'A consensus abrogating' I say: Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) explicitly stated that it is permissible to form consensus contrary to what was previously agreed upon, unless the first is the consensus of the Companions among them; for it is stronger than all other consensuses, and cannot be abrogated by a later consensus, because abrogating the stronger by the weaker is not permissible. (Al-Sunbuli) 'It is permissible to abrogate the Book, likewise the Sunnah, and consensus.' (Al-Qamar) 'Those whose hearts are reconciled etc.' They are those who embraced Islam but had weakness in their Islam and no pure intention; so their hearts are reconciled so that they might be favored by Islam. (Al-Qamar) 'In the Book' Allah Most High said: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor, the needy, those who work on them, and those whose hearts are reconciled' (Surah al-Tawbah: 60). (Al-Muhashi) 'We said that was etc.' meaning that the falling of their share was not by consensus, but rather because the cause of their share was weakness of Islam; when Islam became strong, its cause ceased, and the ruling ends with the ending of its cause, so their share fell. Thus said Ali al-Qari (may Allah have mercy on him). (Al-Qamar) Al-Tabarani narrated in his Tafsir from ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: When ‘Uyayna ibn Hisn came to him [saying] that this religion is the truth from Allah, so whoever wishes may believe and whoever wishes may disbelieve. And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from al-Sha‘bi (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The mu'allafatu qulubuhum existed at the time of the Prophet, and when Abu Bakr succeeded, he cut them off, and consensus was formed on that. In one narration from ‘Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: This is something that the Messenger of Allah used to give you to reconcile your hearts to Islam; now Allah has honored Islam; if you repent that is good, otherwise between us and you is the sword. [Ishraq al-Absar: 26]
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Noor al-Anwar on a section (Fasl) regarding the types of Bayan (clarification), specifically focusing on the topic of naskh (abrogation). The commentator engages with several disputed issues: whether naskh can occur by qiyas (analogical reasoning) or by ijma (consensus). The passage begins by citing a statement attributed to 'Ali (ra) that if religion were by opinion, the inside of the khuff would be more worthy of wiping, but the Prophet wiped over the outside, establishing a sunna that overrides rational preference. The commentator then refutes the view of some Shafi'is (Ibn Surayj and al-Anmati) who permit naskh of the Book and Sunnah by opinion or by qiyas derived from them. The Hanafi position is that naskh requires a textual source of equal or greater authority; qiyas can specify but cannot abrogate. Regarding naskh by ijma, the commentator distinguishes between two statements of Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi: one denying naskh by ijma and another allowing naskh of an earlier ijma by a later ijma. The commentator reconciles this by explaining that ijma cannot abrogate the Book or Sunnah, but a later consensus can abrogate an earlier consensus if the benefit changes, except for the consensus of the Companions, which is stronger. The passage then discusses the case of mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are reconciled), arguing that the cessation of their share in zakat was not by a new ijma abrogating the Quranic verse, but rather by the ending of the cause (weakness of Islam) leading to the expiry of the ruling. The glosses (Al-Qamar, Al-Muhashi, Al-Sunbuli) provide additional clarifications: the definition of naskh, the difference between specification and abrogation, and the strength of different types of consensus.

Key Terms
نَسْخ (naskh): Abrogation: the removal of a ruling by a later revealed text, indicating the expiry of its duration.
قِيَاس (qiyas): Analogical deduction: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
تَخْصِيص (takhsis): Specification: restricting a general text to some of its instances, often by reason or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this lesson, why is abrogation by qiyas invalid?
A) Because qiyas is not a source of law at all.
B) Because naskh requires certain knowledge, and qiyas provides only probable knowledge.
C) Because the Prophet prohibited using qiyas.
D) Because qiyas can only be used in mu'amalat, not in 'ibadat.

Key Takeaway
Naskh by qiyas is invalid in Hanafi usul because abrogation requires certain textual evidence, and qiyas—being probabilistic—cannot override a revealed ruling.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate

Naskh by Ijma: The Hanafi Position and the Case of Mu'allafatu Qulubuhum
Commentary on the invalidity of abrogation by consensus and the case of mu'allafatu qulubuhum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why consensus (ijma) cannot abrogate the Quran or Sunnah according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish between abrogation and the expiry of a ruling due to the cessation of its cause.

Core Idea
Consensus cannot abrogate revelation because it cannot know the duration of divine commands; a ruling may end when its cause ends, which is not abrogation.

This lesson continues the discussion from Noor al-Anwar on the limits of naskh, now focusing on whether consensus (ijma) can abrogate a ruling. The majority position (Jumhur) is that ijma cannot abrogate any evidence, including previous ijma. However, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi introduces a nuance: later consensus can abrogate an earlier consensus unless that earlier consensus is from the Companions. The passage then examines a key historical case: the cessation of the share for mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are reconciled) in zakat. Some argued this was abrogation of the Quranic verse by consensus. The Hanafi response is that the ruling ended because its cause—weakness of Islam—ceased to exist. This is a critical distinction between true naskh and the natural expiry of a rule.

Guided Path
1
State the majority view on naskh by ijma
According to the Jumhur (majority of scholars), consensus cannot abrogate any other evidence—neither the Quran, Sunnah, previous ijma, nor qiyas. This is because ijma is based on opinions gathered from scholars, and opinions cannot determine the beginning or end of divine rulings.
The gloss clarifies that this also means that ijma itself cannot be abrogated unless it is a later ijma abrogating an earlier one, which is a debated point.
2
Examine al-Bazdawi's two statements and their reconciliation
al-Bazdawi (Fakhr al-Islam) said in the chapter on naskh that naskh by ijma is not possible, but in the chapter on ijma, he said that later ijma can abrogate earlier ijma. The commentator reconciles: ijma cannot abrogate the Book or Sunnah, but within the realm of consensuses not based on those sources, a later consensus can overrule an earlier one if the underlying benefit changes. However, the consensus of the Companions is stronger and cannot be abrogated by a weaker later consensus.
This shows that ijma has some legislative force but is subordinate to revelation.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazili argument from the case of mu'allafatu qulubuhum
Some Mu'tazila claimed that the Quranic verse granting zakat to mu'allafatu qulubuhum was abrogated by the consensus of the Companions when Abu Bakr discontinued the practice. They see this as evidence that ijma can abrogate the Quran.
The verse is Surah al-Tawbah 9:60, which lists eight categories of zakat recipients, including mu'allafatu qulubuhum.
4
Apply the Hanafi response: expiry of the cause, not abrogation
The Hanafi response is that the ruling did not end by naskh but by the expiry of its cause (infitah al-illah). The share for mu'allafatu qulubuhum was given because their faith was weak; once Islam became strong under Abu Bakr, the cause ceased, and the ruling naturally ended. This is not abrogation but the termination of a law that was conditional on a specific circumstance.
Alternatively, some narrations suggest that the ruling was abrogated by a hadith that was later forgotten—but even then, it is abrogation by hadith, not by consensus.
5
Derive the usul principle and its practical impact
The key principle: a ruling based on a cause (illa) remains only as long as that cause exists. When the cause disappears, the ruling disappears, but this is not naskh. Naskh is the active removal of a ruling by a new revealed source. This distinction preserves the textual basis for naskh and prevents consensus from overriding divine texts.
This is a foundational Hanafi usul principle: al-hukm yadur ma'a 'illatihi wujudan wa 'adaman (the ruling revolves with its cause, in existence and non-existence).

فصل في أقسام البيان ولا يعرف بالرأي انتهاء الحسن، وقال فخر الإسلام له: يجوز نسخ الإجماع بالإجماع، ولعله أراد به أن الإجماع يتصوّر أن يكون لمصلحة، ثم تتبدّل تلك المصلحة، فينعقد إجماع ناسخ للأول، وعند بعض المعتزلة يجوز نسخ الكتاب بالإجماع؛ لأن المؤلفة قلوبهم

ولا يعرف بالرأي فلا يقدر الأمة على معرفة مدة الحكم، والنسخ بيان مدة بقاء الحكم وكونه حسنًا إلى ذلك
الوقت فكيف يكون الإجماع ناسحًا؟ (القمر) وقال فخر الإسلام : أي البزدوي في باب الإجماع. (القمر) ولعله أراد به إلخ اعلم أن فخر الإسلام قال في باب النسخ: إن النسخ بالإجماع لا يكون، وقال في باب الإجماع: إن نسخ الإجماع بالإجماع جائز، فبين قوليه تدافع فيدفعه الشارح الله بهذا القول، وحاصله: أن الإجماع لا ينعقد بخلاف الكتاب والسنة، فلا يكون ناسخا لهما، وهذا هو المراد مما قال في باب النسخ، وما قال في باب الإجماع فلعله أراد به إلخ. (القمر) فينعقد إجماع ناسخ الخ: لأن حسن الحكم السابق كان بهذه
المصلحة، ولما تبدّلت المصلحة فعلم أنه ليس بحسن، فانعقد إجماع آخر بتوفيق الله تعالى ناسخ الأول.(القمر) إجماع ناسخ: قلت: قد صرّح الإمام فخر الإسلام الله يجوز أن يُجمع على خلاف ما أجمع عليه سابقا إلا أن يكون الأول إجماع الصحابة منه؛ فإنه قوي من سائر الإجماعات، فلا ينسخ بإجماع من بعدهم، فإن إبطال القوي بالأضعف لا يجوز. (السنبلي) يجوز نسخ الكتاب وكذا السنة والإجماع. (القمر) المؤلفة قلوبهم إلخ: هم الذين
أسلموا، وكان في إسلامهم ضعف وليس لهم نية خالصة، فيؤلّف قلوبهم ليكونوا محظوظين بالإسلام.(القمر) في الكتاب: قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ وَالْمَسَاكِينِ وَالْعَامِلِينَ عَلَيْهَا وَالْمُؤَلَّفَةِ قُلُوبُهُمْ﴾ (التوبة: ٦٠) (المحشي) قلنا كان ذلك إلخ يعني أنه ليس سقوط نصيبهم بالإجماع، بل لأن علة نصيبهم كانت ضعف الإسلام، فلما
قوي الإسلام فات علته والحكم ينتهي بانتهاء علته، فسقط نصيبهم. كذا قال العلي القاري له. (القمر) روى الطبراني في تفسيره عن عمر بن الخطاب له أنه قال: لما جاءه عُيينة بن حصين أن هذا الدين حق من الله، فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر و روى ابن أبي شيبه عن الشعبي له قال : كانت المؤلفة قلوبهم في زمن النبي ولما خلف أبو بكر ،قطعهم، وانعقد الإجماع عليه، وفي رواية عن عمر له أنه قال: هذا شيء كان يعطيكم رسول الله له لأن يؤلّف قلوبكم على الإسلام والآن أعزّ الله الإسلام، فإن توبوا فذلك خير، وإلا فبيننا وبينكم السيف. [إشراق الأبصار: ٢٦]
Section on the types of Bayan. Statement on abrogation. Religion [would be] by opinion, then the inside of the khuff would be more deserving of wiping than its outside, but I saw the Messenger of Allah wiping over the outside of the khuff, not its inside. Likewise, consensus is in the meaning of the Book and Sunnah. As for the fact that analogy is not an abrogator of analogy, it is because if two analogies conflict at one time, the mujtahid acts on whichever he wills by the testimony of his heart; and if they are in two times, he acts on the latter analogy among those referred to, but that is not called abrogation in terminology. Ibn Surayj, a follower of al-Shafi‘i, permitted abrogation of the Book and Sunnah by opinion; and al-Anmati among them permitted abrogation of the Book by an analogy derived from it. Likewise, consensus according to the majority is not valid as an abrogator of any evidence because it is an expression of the gathering of opinions. If analogy is not an abrogator of the Book and Sunnah, then it is also not an abrogator of consensus. (Al-Qamar) 'The mujtahid acts on the latter etc.' not because the latter analogy indicates the end of the ruling established by the first analogy, for opinion has no entry into knowing the end of goodness or badness; rather, because he knows at that time that the first analogy was not correct, so he leaves it and does not act on it. (Al-Qamar) 'Abrogation etc.' for lack of truth of the definition of abrogation as mentioned earlier. (Al-Qamar) 'He permits etc.' because abrogation is a clarification like specification; so what is permissible for specification is also permissible for abrogation. We say: The analogy of abrogation to specification has a difference; for the indication of reason can be specifying but cannot be abrogating, so how can they be equal? For specification is clarification, while abrogation is removal and nullification. (Al-Qamar) 'Al-Anmati among them etc.' i.e., Abu al-Qasim al-Anmati from the companions of al-Shafi‘i said: Every analogy derived from the Quran permits abrogation of the Quran by it; likewise every analogy derived from the Sunnah permits abrogation of the Sunnah by it. This is, in reality, abrogation of the Book by the Book and abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah. However, the description by which the branch is referred back to the original text mentioned in the Book and Sunnah is not certain as being the meaning in the ruling established in the text. If that meaning were certain, being specified in the text, then abrogation by it would be permissible just like the text. (Al-Qamar) 'Al-Anmati' in some manuscripts here has a different expression. (Al-Muhashi) 'According to the majority' i.e., it is not an abrogator of any evidence according to the majority, nor can it be abrogated; because the agreement of all on a ruling without temporal limitation indicates that it is good or evil by itself, not susceptible to falling. (Al-Sunbuli) 'Of any evidence' i.e., the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy. (Al-Qamar) 'Because it' i.e., consensus is an expression of gathering of opinions; this is loose; because consensus is transitive, and gathering is intransitive; how is the attribution and interpretation valid? Unless it is taken as an interpretation from the perspective of what results from consensus. (Al-Qamar) Abu Dawud narrated no. 162, chapter on how to wipe, from ‘Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: If religion were by opinion, then the bottom of the khuff would be more deserving of wiping than its top; but I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wiping over the top of his khuff. (Page 598) ... Statement on abrogation. Section on the types of Bayan. And the end of goodness is not known by opinion. And Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is permissible to abrogate consensus by consensus. Perhaps he meant that consensus is conceivable to be for a benefit, then that benefit changes, so a new consensus arises abrogating the first. According to some Mu‘tazila, it is permissible to abrogate the Book by consensus, because 'those whose hearts are reconciled' (mu’allafatu qulubuhum) (may Allah be pleased with them) are mentioned in the Book, and their share of charity fell by the consensus that was formed in the time of Abu Bakr. We said: That was of the type of ending of the ruling due to the ending of its cause. And it was said: That was abrogated by a hadith he narrated during the caliphate of Abu Bakr, and they agreed on its authenticity, but the hadith was forgotten from hearts. (i.e., when opinions gather) And it is not known by opinion, so the ummah cannot know the duration of the ruling. And abrogation is a clarification of the duration of the ruling and its being good up to that time; so how can consensus be an abrogator? (Al-Qamar) 'And Fakhr al-Islam said' i.e., al-Bazdawi in the chapter on consensus. (Al-Qamar) 'Perhaps he meant etc.' Know that Fakhr al-Islam said in the chapter on abrogation that abrogation by consensus does not occur, and he said in the chapter on consensus that abrogation of consensus by consensus is permissible; so there is contradiction between his statements. The commentator (al-Sharh) deflects this with this saying. Its summary: That consensus cannot be formed contrary to the Book and Sunnah, so it cannot abrogate them; that is the meaning of what he said in the chapter on abrogation. And what he said in the chapter on consensus perhaps he meant etc. (Al-Qamar) 'Then a new consensus arises abrogating etc.' Because the goodness of the previous ruling was due to that benefit; and when the benefit changed, it was known that it is not good, so another consensus arose by Allah's grace abrogating the first. (Al-Qamar) 'A consensus abrogating' I say: Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) explicitly stated that it is permissible to form consensus contrary to what was previously agreed upon, unless the first is the consensus of the Companions among them; for it is stronger than all other consensuses, and cannot be abrogated by a later consensus, because abrogating the stronger by the weaker is not permissible. (Al-Sunbuli) 'It is permissible to abrogate the Book, likewise the Sunnah, and consensus.' (Al-Qamar) 'Those whose hearts are reconciled etc.' They are those who embraced Islam but had weakness in their Islam and no pure intention; so their hearts are reconciled so that they might be favored by Islam. (Al-Qamar) 'In the Book' Allah Most High said: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor, the needy, those who work on them, and those whose hearts are reconciled' (Surah al-Tawbah: 60). (Al-Muhashi) 'We said that was etc.' meaning that the falling of their share was not by consensus, but rather because the cause of their share was weakness of Islam; when Islam became strong, its cause ceased, and the ruling ends with the ending of its cause, so their share fell. Thus said Ali al-Qari (may Allah have mercy on him). (Al-Qamar) Al-Tabarani narrated in his Tafsir from ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: When ‘Uyayna ibn Hisn came to him [saying] that this religion is the truth from Allah, so whoever wishes may believe and whoever wishes may disbelieve. And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from al-Sha‘bi (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The mu'allafatu qulubuhum existed at the time of the Prophet, and when Abu Bakr succeeded, he cut them off, and consensus was formed on that. In one narration from ‘Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: This is something that the Messenger of Allah used to give you to reconcile your hearts to Islam; now Allah has honored Islam; if you repent that is good, otherwise between us and you is the sword. [Ishraq al-Absar: 26]
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Noor al-Anwar on a section (Fasl) regarding the types of Bayan (clarification), specifically focusing on the topic of naskh (abrogation). The commentator engages with several disputed issues: whether naskh can occur by qiyas (analogical reasoning) or by ijma (consensus). The passage begins by citing a statement attributed to 'Ali (ra) that if religion were by opinion, the inside of the khuff would be more worthy of wiping, but the Prophet wiped over the outside, establishing a sunna that overrides rational preference. The commentator then refutes the view of some Shafi'is (Ibn Surayj and al-Anmati) who permit naskh of the Book and Sunnah by opinion or by qiyas derived from them. The Hanafi position is that naskh requires a textual source of equal or greater authority; qiyas can specify but cannot abrogate. Regarding naskh by ijma, the commentator distinguishes between two statements of Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi: one denying naskh by ijma and another allowing naskh of an earlier ijma by a later ijma. The commentator reconciles this by explaining that ijma cannot abrogate the Book or Sunnah, but a later consensus can abrogate an earlier consensus if the benefit changes, except for the consensus of the Companions, which is stronger. The passage then discusses the case of mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are reconciled), arguing that the cessation of their share in zakat was not by a new ijma abrogating the Quranic verse, but rather by the ending of the cause (weakness of Islam) leading to the expiry of the ruling. The glosses (Al-Qamar, Al-Muhashi, Al-Sunbuli) provide additional clarifications: the definition of naskh, the difference between specification and abrogation, and the strength of different types of consensus.

Key Terms
إِجْمَاع (ijma): Consensus of the Muslim scholars on a ruling after the death of the Prophet.
مُؤَلَّفَةُ قُلُوبِهِم (mu'allafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are reconciled; a category of zakat recipients whose faith is weak and are given to strengthen their attachment to Islam.
عِلَّة (illa): Effective cause or ratio legis; the characteristic that grounds a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the claim that the share of mu'allafatu qulubuhum was abrogated by consensus?
A) Because consensus cannot be formed on abrogation.
B) Because the ruling ended due to the expiration of its cause (weakness of Islam).
C) Because the Quranic verse was later abrogated by another verse.
D) Because the Prophet himself revoked the share before his death.

Key Takeaway
Consensus cannot abrogate divine texts; apparent cases of abrogation often involve the expiry of a ruling due to the cessation of its cause, a distinction central to Hanafi usul.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
10 min
Taqlid of a Companion: Binding Nature and Conditions
Explains when a Companion's opinion becomes binding and the disagreement on taqlid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a Companion's opinion becomes binding ijma and evaluate the Hanafi position on taqlid of a Companion.

Core Idea
A Companion's opinion becomes binding ijma only when it reaches another Companion who remains silent; otherwise, taqlid is disputed.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar addresses the legal weight of a Companion's statement. Scholars differ on whether it is obligatory to follow it without evidence. The text explains that if the statement reaches another Companion and he remains silent, it transforms into ijma, a binding proof. It then uses the example of guarantee for a shared worker to illustrate how Abu Hanifa and his students applied this principle differently, and clarifies that in widespread matters, even a Companion's opinion may not override common practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ikhtilaf
Determine the dispute: Is it obligatory to follow a single Companion's opinion when it has not been opposed by another Companion? Note the two positions and who holds them.
The passage explicitly mentions 'scholars differed regarding taqlid of him: some make taqlid of him, and some do not.'
2
Determine the condition for ijma
Identify what happens when the statement reaches another Companion. What is the effect of silence? How does it create binding ijma?
The passage says: 'If he remained silent, it is ijma.'
3
Analyze the guarantee example
Examine the dispute among the Hanafi imams on the liability of a shared worker (ajir mushtarak) for theft. How does the Sahibayn's reliance on Ali's ruling demonstrate taqlid of a Companion? Why did Abu Hanifa not follow it?
The text suggests Abu Hanifa may have had evidence that Ali retracted or that it was a conciliation.
4
Recognize the exception for 'amm al-balwa
Identify the condition that limits the application of a Companion's opinion: when the matter is widespread in calamity, even silence does not create ijma. Why does this exception exist?
The passage: 'this disagreement is specific to what is not widespread in calamity.'

وهذا الاختلاف المذكور بين العلماء في وجوب التقليد وعدمه في كل ما ثبت عنهم من غير أي تقليد الصحابي خلاف بينهم، ومن غير أن يثبت أن ذلك بلغ غير قائله فسكت مسلما له، يعني في كل ما أي قول الصحابي أي ذلك الغير قال صحابي له قولا، ولم يبلغ غيره من الصحابة ، فحينئذ اختلف العلماء في تقليده، بعضهم يقلدونه، وبعضهم لا، وأما إذا بلغ صحابيًا آخر فإنه لا يخلو، إما أن يسكت هذا الآخر مسلمًا له أو خالفه، فإن سكت كان إجماعًا، فلا يضمن فإن الضمان إما ضمان جبر، فهو يجب بالتعدّي والتفويت، ولم يوجد من الأجير المشترك، وإما ضمان شرط، وهو يجب بالعقد، و لم يوجد عقد موجب لاعتماد ولا ثالث للضمان فكان الشيء أمانة في يده. (القمر) كالأجير الخاص وهو الذي ورد العقد على منافعه مطلقا، وهو يستحق الأجر بتسليم نفسه مدة الإجارة، وسُمّي به؛ لأنه لا يقدر على أن يعمل لغيره. (القمر) لما ضاع في يده فلا ضمان عليه، كذا ههنا. (القمر) فهو أي أبو حنيفة له أخذ بالرأي، وأما علي الله فلعله أنما ضمن الخياطة بطريق الصلح لا بطريق الحكم الشرعي، والفتوى على قول الإمام كذا قال قاضي خان، وذكر الزيلعي أن الفتوى على قولهما، كذا في "فتح الغفار". قال العيني في شرح "الكنز": بقول الصاحبين يُفتي بعضهم، وبقول الإمام آخرون. (القمر) كالحريق الغالب إلخ ودليل الصاحبين في تضمينهما في السرقة قول على كرم الله وجهه رواه ابن أبي شيبة، وروى الشافعي الله عنه أنه كان يضمن الصبّاغ والصائغ، ويقول: لا يصلح الناس إلا ذلك، فكأن أبا حنيفة يسقيه في هذه المسألة لم يقلّد عليا ، والصاحبان قلداه، لكن قال الشيخ عبد الحق المحدث الدهلوي له في "فتح المنان في تأييد مذهب النعمان": قال ابن المبارك : قال أبو حنيفة لله ما جاء من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فبالرأس والعين، وما جاء عن أصحابه فلا أتركه، فهذا نص صريح منه على أن يقلد الصحابة منه، وأما عمله في بعض المسائل على خلاف قول الصحابي الله فلعله ثبت عنده معارضة قول آخر كما قيل في مسألة التضمين: إن أمير المؤمنين عليا هيه رجع عنه، بل نقل فيه حديثا مرفوعًا، فافهم. (السنبلي) في كل ما ثبت أي في كل حكم ثبت عن الصحابة له. (القمر) في كل ما إلخ وأيضا هذا الاختلاف مخصوص بما لم يعمّ ،بلواه، وأما ما عمّ البلوى فيه و ورد قول الصحابي الله مخالفا لعمل المبتلين لا يجب الأخذ به بالاتفاق؛ لأنه لا يقبل فيه السنة أيضًا. (السنبلي) بلغ صحابيًا :آخر أي تحقيقا أو دلالةً بأن كانت الحادثة مما لا يحتمل الخفاء عليهم لعموم البلوى وحاجة الكل كذا قيل. (القمر) فإن سكت أي إن سكت مسلما له، وظهر نقل هذا القول في التابعين ولم يُرو خلاف عن غيره كان إجماعا، فيجب إلخ. (القمر)
And this disagreement mentioned among scholars regarding the obligation of taqlid or not in everything established from them without any [explanation] – taqlid of the Companion – is a difference among them. And without it being established that that reached someone other than its speaker and he remained silent conceding to it, meaning in everything – i.e., the statement of the Companion – i.e., that other – a Companion said a statement, and it did not reach another Companion. Then the scholars differed regarding taqlid of him: some make taqlid of him, and some do not. But if it reached another Companion, then it is either that this other remained silent conceding to him or he opposed him. If he remained silent, it is ijma. So he does not guarantee, for guarantee is either guarantee of reparation, which is due by transgression and destruction, and it is not found in the shared worker; or guarantee of condition, which is due by contract, and there is no contract requiring reliance, and there is no third type of guarantee, so the item is a trust in his hand. (al-Qamar) Like the private employee, who is one whose contract is on his benefits absolutely, and he deserves the wage by surrendering himself for the duration of the lease, and he is called that because he cannot work for others. (al-Qamar) When lost in his hand, there is no guarantee upon him, likewise here. (al-Qamar) So he – i.e., Abu Hanifa – took by opinion. As for Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), perhaps he imposed guarantee on the tailor by way of conciliation, not by legal ruling. And the fatwa is according to the Imam's statement; thus said Qadhi Khan, and al-Zayla'i mentioned that the fatwa is according to their statement, thus in Fath al-Ghaffar. Al-Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz: By the statement of the two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) some give fatwa, and by the Imam's statement others. (al-Qamar) Like the prevalent fire etc. And the proof of the two Companions in imposing guarantee in theft is the statement of Ali (karramallahu wajhah) narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. And al-Shafi'i narrated from him that he used to impose guarantee on the dyer and the goldsmith, and said: People cannot be set right except by that. So it is as if Abu Hanifa in this issue did not make taqlid of Ali, and the two Companions made taqlid of him. But Shaykh Abd al-Haqq al-Muhaddith al-Dihlawi said in Fath al-Mannan fi Ta'yid Madhhab al-Nu'man: Ibn al-Mubarak said: Abu Hanifa said: Whatever has come from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is with head and eye, and whatever has come from his Companions, I do not abandon it. This is a clear text from him that he makes taqlid of the Companions. As for his action in some issues contrary to the statement of a Companion, perhaps it is established with him a conflicting statement of another, as was said in the issue of guarantee: The Commander of the Faithful Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) retracted from it, rather he narrated a marfu' hadith about it. So understand. (al-Sanbali) In everything established – i.e., in every ruling established from the Companions. (al-Qamar) In everything etc. Also this disagreement is specific to what is not widespread in calamity. As for what is widespread in calamity, and the statement of a Companion comes contrary to the practice of those afflicted, it is not obligatory to take it by consensus, because even the Sunnah is not accepted in that. (al-Sanbali) Reached another Companion – i.e., actually or by indication, if the incident is not likely to be hidden from them due to the widespread calamity and the need of all, thus it is said. (al-Qamar) For if he remained silent – i.e., if he remained silent conceding to it, and the transmission of this statement appeared among the Tabi'un and no opposition from others is narrated, it is ijma, so it is obligatory, etc. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle of taqlid of a Companion (sahabi). It begins by stating the ikhtilaf among scholars on whether it is obligatory to follow a Companion's opinion when his statement alone is known and no other Companion has opposed it. Some say it is obligatory, others not. However, if the statement reaches another Companion and he remains silent without objection, it becomes ijma (consensus), which is binding. The commentary then uses the example of the guarantee (daman) of a shared worker (ajir mushtarak) to illustrate the ikhtilaf. Abu Hanifa held that such a worker is not liable, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held him liable in cases of theft, relying on the practice of Caliph Ali. The passage explains that Abu Hanifa's reluctance to follow Ali's ruling may be due to a reported retraction by Ali or because he had evidence that the ruling was based on conciliation rather than legal determination. The author then cites the statement of Abu Hanifa: 'Whatever comes from the Prophet I accept unconditionally; whatever comes from his Companions I do not abandon,' proving his adherence to taqlid of Companions. The passage further restricts the ikhtilaf to matters that are not widespread (ghayr 'amm al-balwa). If a Companion's opinion opposes the common practice of the people in widespread matters, it is not obligatory to follow it, as even the Sunnah is not accepted in such cases. Finally, the passage clarifies that if a Companion's statement reaches another Companion and he remains silent, and the statement is transmitted among the Tabi'un without known opposition, it becomes ijma and is binding.

Key Terms
تقليد الصحابي (taqlid al-sahabi): Following the legal opinion of a Companion without demanding evidence from Quran and Sunnah.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the Companions; binding proof.
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage.
عام البلوى (amm al-balwa): A matter of widespread occurrence and general need, such that it cannot be hidden from the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a Companion's statement reaches another Companion and he remains silent, what is the legal effect?
A) It is not considered at all
B) It becomes ijma and is binding
C) It is considered a weak proof
D) It is only binding if the second Companion explicitly agrees

Key Takeaway
A Companion's opinion becomes binding ijma only when it reaches another Companion who remains silent; otherwise, it is a matter of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
6 min
The Hanafi Definition of Ijma' and Its Pillar
Technical definition and explanation of the rukn of ijma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ijma', identify its four key qualifications, and explain the two forms of its pillar (rukn) as either explicit declaration or collective action.

Core Idea
Ijma' is the unanimous agreement of all qualified mujtahids of the Ummah on any matter not definitively settled by Quran or Sunnah.

In this lesson, we dissect the technical definition of ijma' (consensus) as presented by the Hanafi commentator al-Qamar. Understanding this definition precisely is crucial because it determines whose agreement is binding and on what matters. We will also explore the pillar (rukn) of ijma'—how it manifests in both words and actions—and examine historical examples that illustrate the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Linguistic and Technical Meanings
The word 'ijma' linguistically means agreement (al-ittifaq) and also determination (al-'azm). The technical definition adds conditions: agreement of righteous mujtahids from the ummah of Muhammad on a matter that is verbal or actual, and not already definitively established by the Book and Sunnah.
Note the dual linguistic root: from jama'a (to gather), implying both gathering thoughts (determination) and gathering opinions (agreement).
2
Break Down the Four Qualifications in the Definition
The definition contains four restrictions: (1) mujtahids only (excluding muqallids), (2) righteous (excluding those who follow desires or are sinful), (3) from the ummah of Muhammad (excluding previous communities), and (4) the matter agreed upon is not cut off (qat'i) from the Book or Sunnah. These ensure that ijma' is authoritative and represents true scholarly consensus.
The term 'mujtahid' is technical—a scholar qualified to derive rulings directly from the sources.
3
Understand the Scope of the Agreed Matter
The agreed matter can be shar'i, rational, or customary. Some scholars restrict ijma' to shar'i matters only, but the Hanafi view (following Ibn al-Hajib) includes rational and customary matters as well, as long as they are not already definitively settled. This broadens the application of ijma' to areas like politics and customary practices.
This is a point of ikhtilaf with the author of al-Tawdih, who restricted ijma' to shar'i rulings.
4
Analyze the Two Forms of the Pillar (Rukn)
The rukn of ijma' is either (a) an explicit verbal agreement, e.g., 'We have all agreed on this ruling,' or (b) a collective action, e.g., all mujtahids engaging in mudarabah (profit-sharing) demonstrates their consensus on its permissibility. The text emphasizes that both are valid manifestations of consensus.
The example of the Companions' bay'ah to Abu Bakr (radhiAllahu anhu) is given as a practical precedent for action-based ijma'.
5
Apply the Definition to Historical Examples
The passage cites two examples: the consensus on the caliphate of Abu Bakr (action-based: all Companions pledged allegiance) and the consensus on the legality of mudarabah, muzara'ah, and partnership (practice-based: all mujtahids engaged in these contracts). These illustrate how the rukn operates in practice.
These examples show that ijma' is not merely theoretical but was exercised by the early community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإجماع تعريف الإجماع وركنه ولما فرغ عن أقسام السنة شرع في بيان الإجماع فقال: تعريف الاجماع وركنه ] باب الإجماع وهو في اللغة الاتفاق وفي الشريعة اتفاق مجتهدين صالحين من أمة محمد علي واحد على أمر قولي أو فعلي. ركن الإجماع نوعان: عزيمة: وهو التكلّم ما منهم يوجب أهل الإجماع في عصر الاتفاق، أي اتفاق الكل على أي ما يقوم به الإجماع أي أصل أي العزيمة الحكم بأن يقولوا أجمعنا على هذا إن كان ذلك الشيء من باب القول. أو شروعهم في الفعل إن كان من بابه أي كان ذالك الشيء من باب الفعل كما إذا شرع أهل الاجتهاد جميعًا في المضاربة أو المزارعة أو الشركة كان ذالك إجماعا منهم على شرعيتها. أهله وهو في اللغة إلخ: قلت: قال بعض المحققين: الإجماع لغةً العزم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَأَجْمِعُوا أَمْرَكُمْ، (يونس: ٧١) وفي قوله : " لا صيام لمن لم يجمع الصيام من الليل والاتفاق، وكلاهما أي الذي بمعنى العزم والذي بمعنى الاتفاق مأخوذان من الجمع، فإن العزم فيه جمع الخواطر والاتفاق فيه جمع الخواطر (السنبلي) اتفاق مجتهدين إلخ المراد بالاتفاق الاشتراك في الاعتقاد أو القول أو الفعل، والأولى أن يقول: "هو الاتفاق في كل عصر على أمر على أمر من الأمور من جميع من هو من هذه الأمة ليشمل المجتهدين في أمر يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، ويشمل المجتهدين والعوام فيما لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، فيصير التعريف حينئذٍ جامعا ومانعا، والمراد بالمجتهدين جميع المجتهدين الكائنين في عصر من الأعصار واحترز به عن اتفاق المقلدين واحترز بقوله: "صالحين" عن اتفاق مجتهدين ذوي هوى أو فاسقين وبقوله: "من أمة محمد" اتفاق مجتهدي الشرائع السابقة. (القمر) قولي أو فعلي شرعي أو عقلي أو عرفي غير ثابت بالكتاب والسنة قطعًا، وأطلق الأمر اتباعًا لابن الحاجب، ولم كما خصص صاحب "التوضيح" تنبيها على أنه يجب اتباع اجتماع المجتهدين في الحكم الغير أيضا كأمر الحروب ونحوها. (القمر) أو شروعهم إلخ وهذا كالإجماع على خلافة الصديق الله، فإن الصحابة بايعوا بأيديهم، وأقروا بألسنتهم. (القمر) المضاربة أو المزارعة أو الشركة: المضاربة عقد شركة في الربح بمال من جانب وعمل من جانب والمزارعة عقد على الزرع ببعض الخارج، والشركة عبارة عن عقد بين المتشاركين في الأصل والربح كذا في "الدر المختار". (القمر) يخصصه بالشرعي عن الشرعي
Chapter on Consensus: Definition of Consensus and its Pillar. When he finished the sections of the Sunnah, he began to explain consensus and said: 'Definition of consensus and its pillar.' Chapter on Consensus: It is in language agreement, and in sacred law the agreement of righteous mujtahids of the nation of Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) on a single matter, whether verbal or actual. The pillar of consensus is of two types: Resolution: which is that they speak what obligates the people of consensus in the era of agreement, meaning the agreement of all on that which establishes consensus, i.e., the origin, i.e., the resolution of the ruling by saying 'We have agreed on this' if that thing is of the category of speech. Or their commencing the action if it is of its category, i.e., if that thing is of the category of action, such as when all the people of ijtihad commence in mudarabah (profit-sharing), muzara'ah (sharecropping), or partnership, that is a consensus from them on its legality. Its People: And it is in language etc. I say: Some investigators said: Consensus in language is determination (al-'azm) as in His statement (Exalted): 'So determine your plan' (Yunus 71) and in his statement (peace be upon him): 'No fasting for the one who does not determine the fast from the night.' And agreement (al-ittifaq), both meanings (i.e., determination and agreement) are derived from gathering (al-jam'). For determination involves gathering thoughts, and agreement involves gathering thoughts. (al-Sunbuli) 'Agreement of mujtahids etc.' The intended meaning of agreement is participation in belief, speech, or action. It is better to say: 'It is agreement in every era on a matter of matters from all who are of this nation' to include the mujtahids in a matter needing independent reasoning, and to include the mujtahids and commoners in matters not needing independent reasoning. Thus the definition becomes comprehensive and exclusive. 'Mujtahids' means all mujtahids existing in any era, and this excludes the agreement of muqallids (followers). His statement 'righteous' excludes the agreement of mujtahids who follow desires or are sinners. His statement 'of the nation of Muhammad' excludes the agreement of mujtahids of previous laws. (al-Qamar) 'Verbal or actual' – shar'i, rational, or customary, not established by the Book and Sunnah definitively. He left the matter general following Ibn al-Hajib; and he did not specify it as the author of al-Tawdih did, as a reminder that it is obligatory to follow the agreement of mujtahids even in non-shar'i matters like matters of war and the like. (al-Qamar) 'Or their commencing etc.' This is like the consensus on the caliphate of al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him), for the Companions pledged with their hands and affirmed with their tongues. (al-Qamar) 'Mudarabah, muzara'ah, or partnership': Mudarabah is a contract of partnership in profit with capital from one side and work from the other; muzara'ah is a contract on cultivation for a share of the produce; partnership (shirkah) is a contract between partners in the capital and profit. So it is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. (al-Qamar) He restricts it to the shar'i from the shar'i? [incomplete]
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is a technical definition of ijma' (consensus) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It begins by stating the chapter heading and then gives the linguistic meaning: agreement (al-ittifaq). The shar'i definition is: agreement of all righteous mujtahids from the ummah of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) on a matter, whether it is a statement or an action. The definition carefully restricts the scope: only mujtahids are considered (excluding commoners and muqallids), they must be righteous (excluding those who follow desires or are sinners), and it must be from the ummah of Muhammad (excluding previous prophets' communities). The matter agreed upon can be verbal or actual, and includes shar'i, rational, or customary matters not definitively established by the Book and Sunnah. The author notes that the definition is based on Ibn al-Hajib's formulation but is broader, as emphasized by the commentator al-Sunbuli. The pillar (rukn) of ijma' is twofold: either an explicit statement of agreement (e.g., 'we have agreed on this') or a collective action (e.g., all mujtahids engaging in mudarabah shows their consensus on its legality). Examples from the Companions include the election of Abu Bakr as caliph, which was a consensus by action. The text also clarifies that ijma' can occur in matters of worldly affairs like war, following the view of al-Tawdih. The passage underscores that ijma' is a binding source of law in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma): Agreement of all righteous mujtahids of the ummah of Muhammad on a shar'i, rational, or customary matter.
ركن (rukn): The essential component that constitutes ijma' – either verbal declaration (explicit statement) or collective action (implicit agreement).
مجتهد (mujtahid): A scholar qualified to perform ijtihad, i.e., to derive rulings from the sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The collective silence of all mujtahids on an issue.

Key Takeaway
Ijma' is the unanimous agreement of all qualified mujtahids of the Ummah, established either by explicit declaration or by collective practice, on matters open to ijtihad.

Silent Consensus: Definition and Hanafi Proof
Defines silent consensus and proves its validity with rational and hadith evidence, while addressing the Shafi'i counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define silent consensus (ijma sukuti) and explain why it is binding according to the Hanafi school, while identifying the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Silent consensus is binding because a just scholar cannot remain silent about falsehood; silence equals consent when ability to object exists.

This passage tackles a critical source of law: silent consensus. Unlike explicit consensus, it relies on the silence of scholars after a period of reflection. Understanding when silence constitutes agreement is essential for determining the weight of scholarly opinions. The Hanafi school accepts it as binding, while the Shafi'i school raises the possibility that silence may be due to awe. The passage provides rational proof and a hadith to support the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Locate the definition of silent consensus (ijma sukuti) in the passage. Note its components: some scholars express an opinion, others remain silent after a period of reflection, and no objection occurs.
The period of reflection is typically three days or the duration of a gathering, but most Hanafis leave it to custom (urf).
2
Understand the Rational Proof
The passage argues that a just person cannot remain silent about a wrong he is able to change. Therefore, silence must indicate agreement, otherwise it would be sinful. Grasp the logical chain: ability to object + silence → consent.
This is a purely rational (aqli) proof, not dependent on transmitted texts alone.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Objection
The Shafi'i school objects using the example of Ibn Abbas, who disagreed with Umar on the inheritance increase (al-awl) but remained silent due to awe (haybah). This shows silence may not indicate consent.
Understand the nature of the objection: it challenges the inference from silence to agreement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response refutes the validity of the Ibn Abbas example, stating that Ibn Abbas was a child at the time and thus not a responsible scholar, and Umar was known to welcome the truth. The prophetic hadith 'The silent about the truth is a silent devil' also condemns silence in the face of falsehood.
The response also clarifies that if conclusive evidence of agreement exists (e.g., repeated incidents with no objection), then all schools accept it.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical difference: For Hanafis, a silent consensus from qualified scholars is binding and serves as a source of law. For Shafi'is, it is not binding unless there is further proof that agreement occurred.
This difference affects how legal rulings are derived in cases where only tacit agreement is recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإجماع
تعريف الإجماع وركنه
ورخصة: وهو أن يتكلّم أو يفعل البعض دون البعض، أي يتفق بعضهم على قول أو فعل وسكت الباقون منهم ولا يردّون عليهم بعد مضي مدة التأمل، وهي ثلاثة أيام أو مجلس العلم، ويسمى هذا إجماعًا سكوتيا، وهو مقبول عندنا.
خلافا الشافعي له؛ لأن السكوت كما يكون للموافقة يكون للمهابة، ولا يدلّ على الرضا كما روي عن ابن عباس هما أنه خالف عمر له في مسألة العول، فقيل له: هلا أظهرت حجتك على عمر ؟ فقال: كان رجلاً مهيبا فهبته ومنعتني درته، *
وسكت أي بعد بلوغ الخبر إليهم (القمر) وهي ثلاثة أيام لأن هذا القدر هو المشروع في إظهار العذر، وعند أكثر الحنفية لم تقدّر مدة التأمّل بشيء بل لا بد من مرور أوقات يعلم عادةً أنه لو كان هناك مخالف لأظهر هذا الخلاف . (القمر) ويسمى إلخ فإن هذا السكوت دليل الاتفاق عندنا؛ لأن عدم النهي عن المنكر والسكوت عليه مع القدرة عليه لا يمكن من العدل؛ لأنه فسق، فهذا إجماع ضروري للاحتراز عن نسبتهم إلى الفسق، ألا ترى أن المعتاد أن الكبار يتولون أمر الفتوى والصغار يتبعونهم ويسلّمون قولهم.(القمر) خلافا الشافعي له قيل: إن هذا الخلاف فيما إذا لم يتحقق مع السكوت قرينة قاطعة على الموافقة، وأما إذا قامت القرينة الكذائية كتكرّر وقوع الحادثة بمرات وسكوت الباقين وعدم الإنكار أصلاً فهذا السكوت دليل الموافقة عند الكل، ولا خفاء فيه (القمر)
أن
على الرضا فكيف يكون الإجماع السكوتي حجة مع وقوع الاحتمالات. (القمر) كما روي عن إلخ : قال العلي القاري حه وتفصيله ما ذكره الإمام سراج الدين في شرحه للفرائض من العول ثابت على قول عامة الصحابة محمد باطل عند ابن عباس ما، وهو يدخل النقض على البنات وبنات الابن والأخوات لأب وأم أو لأب، مثاله: زوج، وأم، وأخت لأب وأم، فعند العامة المسألة من ستة، وتعول إلى ثمانية وعند ابن عباس للزوج النصف ثلاثة، وللأم الثلث اثنان، وللأخت الباقي، وهذه أول حادثة وقعت في نوبة عمر له فأشار إلى العباس أن يقسم المال على سهامهم فقبلوا منه ولم ينكره أحد، وكان ابن عباس صبيا، فلما بلغ خالف وقال: ليس في المال نصفان وثلث، فقيل: هلا قلت ذلك في عهد عمر ؟ قال: كنت صبيا، وكان عمر رجلاً مهيبا فهبت والعول هو زيادة سهام الورثة إذا كثرت الفروض على مخرج السهام المفروضة الذي يقال له أصل المسألة (القمر)
أخرجه البيهقي عنه في حديث طويل، وفيه فقيل له: رأيك مع الجماعة أحب إلينا من رأيك منفردًا. [إشراق الأبصار ٢٨]
والجواب أن هذا غير صحيح؛ لأن عمر رضي عنه كان أشد انقيادًا لاستماع الحق من غيره حتى كان يقول: لا خير فيكم ما لم تقولوا، ولا خير لي ما لم أسمع وكيف يُظنّ في حق التقصير في أمور الدين والسكوت عن الحق في موضع الحاجة وقد قال عليها:
الصحابة
** !!
الساكت عن الحق شيطان أخرس".
Chapter of Consensus. Definition of Consensus and Its Pillar. And a license: that some speak or act without others, i.e., some agree on a statement or action and the rest remain silent and do not object after the passage of the period of reflection, which is three days or the time of a scholarly gathering. This is called silent consensus, and it is accepted by us. Contrary to al-Shafi'i, because silence can be due to agreement or due to awe, and does not indicate satisfaction, as narrated from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) that he disagreed with Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) in the issue of al-awl, and it was said to him: 'Why did you not present your proof to Umar?' He said: 'He was an awe-inspiring man, so I feared him, and his whip prevented me.' And he remained silent after the news reached them (al-Qamar?) for three days because this period is prescribed for presenting an excuse. According to most Hanafis, the period of reflection is not specified by a duration, rather it is necessary that enough time passes such that it is customarily known that if there had been an objector, he would have expressed his objection. (al-Qamar) And it is called etc., for this silence is evidence of agreement according to us; because refraining from forbidding the wrong and remaining silent upon it while having the ability to do so is not possible from a just person, because it would be sinful. So this is a necessary consensus to avoid attributing sinfulness to them. Do you not see that the norm is that senior scholars undertake the matter of fatwa, and junior scholars follow them and submit to their opinion. (al-Qamar) Contrary to al-Shafi'i, it is said that this disagreement is when there is no conclusive evidence of agreement accompanying the silence. But if such evidence exists, like the repeated occurrence of an incident multiple times with the rest remaining silent and never objecting, then this silence is evidence of agreement according to all, and there is no concealment in it. (al-Qamar) So how can silent consensus be a proof while probabilities exist? (al-Qamar) As narrated from, etc.: al-Ali al-Qari, may Allah have mercy on him, said and its detail is what Imam Siraj al-Din mentioned in his commentary on the law of inheritance: al-awl is established according to the opinion of the generality of the Companions, but invalid according to Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him). And it involves reduction to daughters, granddaughters, sisters from father and mother or from father. Example: husband, mother, and sister from father and mother. According to the generality, the base is six and increases to eight. According to Ibn Abbas, husband has half = three, mother has one third = two, sister has the remainder. This is the first incident that occurred in the time of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him). He suggested to Abbas to divide the property according to their shares, and they accepted from him and no one objected. Ibn Abbas was a child. When he grew up, he opposed and said: 'There are not two halves and a third in the estate.' It was said: 'Why did you not say that in the time of Umar?' He said: 'I was a child, and Umar was an awe-inspiring man, so I feared him.' And al-awl is the increase of shares of heirs when the shares exceed the denominator used to generate the original shares. (al-Qamar) Al-Bayhaqi narrated it from him in a long hadith, and in it: It was said to him: 'Your opinion with the community is more beloved to us than your opinion alone.' [Ishraq al-Absar 28] And the answer is that this is not correct; because Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) was more inclined to listen to the truth than others, such that he used to say: 'There is no good in you if you do not say (the truth), and no good in me if I do not listen.' And how could it be thought of him that he would fall short in religious matters and remain silent about the truth when needed, especially when he (the Prophet) said: The Companions! The silent about the truth is a silent devil.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of silent consensus (ijma sukuti) and its proof. The author defines it as agreement of some scholars on an opinion or action while others remain silent without objection after a period of reflection (usually three days or the duration of a scholarly gathering). This type of consensus is accepted by the Hanafis. The proof is rational: if a scholar who is able to object does not, then their silence indicates agreement, because remaining silent upon falsehood while having the ability to forbid it would be sinful. This is argued by analogy: just as senior scholars guide junior ones, the silence of the latter implies consent. The passage then addresses the Shafi'i objection that silence may be due to awe (haybah) rather than agreement, citing the case of Ibn Abbas who disagreed with Umar on the inheritance increase (al-awl) but did not speak out due to Umar's awe. The Hanafi response is that this case is not applicable because Umar was known for welcoming the truth, as he himself said, 'There is no good in you if you do not say (the truth).' Moreover, the Prophet said, 'The silent about the truth is a silent devil.' Thus, silence can only be interpreted as agreement when there is no fear or other constraint. The passage also clarifies the duration of the reflection period: according to most Hanafis, it is not fixed but rather determined by custom (urf). Finally, the Hanafi position is that if the silence is accompanied by conclusive evidence of agreement, then all schools accept it as binding.

Key Terms
إجماع سكوتي (ijma sukuti): Consensus where some scholars express an opinion and others remain silent without objection after a period of reflection, indicating tacit agreement.
مدة التأمل (muddat al-ta'ammul): The period allowed for scholars to reflect and object; typically three days or the duration of a scholarly gathering, but according to most Hanafis, it is determined by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why must silent consensus be binding?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly commanded it.
B) Because silence from objection while able indicates agreement, otherwise the silent one would be sinful.
C) Because Ibn Abbas remained silent out of awe, showing agreement.
D) Because the period of reflection is fixed as three days.

Key Takeaway
Silent consensus is binding in the Hanafi school because silence in the face of falsehood while able to object is sinful, thus indicating agreement.

Who Counts in Ijma'? The Mujtahid Condition and the Exclusion of Laypeople
Definition of Ahl al-Ijma' with conditions and a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for membership in Ahl al-Ijma' according to the Hanafi school, distinguish between ijtihadi and non-ijtihadi matters, and evaluate al-Baqillani's objection.

Core Idea
Only a mujtahid who is righteous can participate in Ijma'; laypeople are excluded in ijtihadi matters.

In this passage from Noor al-Anwar, the author defines the qualifications for Ahl al-Ijma'. The key conditions are ijtihad and righteousness (`adalah). The passage clarifies that in matters requiring independent reasoning (ijtihad), only qualified mujtahids count; in definitive matters (like the number of rak`ahs), all Muslims must agree. We also encounter Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's view that laypeople can form Ijma' even in ijtihadi matters, and the Hanafi response that laypeople must simply follow scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditions
Read the first part of the passage. What two conditions does the author state for a person to be part of Ahl al-Ijma'?
Look for the words 'mujtahid' and 'salih' (righteous).
2
Distinguish Ijtihadi vs Non-Ijtihadi Matters
The passage says 'except in what is independent of ijtihad'. List the examples given for non-ijtihadi matters. Why must all Muslims agree in such matters?
Examples: transmission of Quran, numbers of rak`ahs, amounts of zakat, borrowing bread, bathing.
3
Locate the Baqillani Dispute
Find where al-Baqillani's opinion is mentioned. What does he claim?
He says ijtihad is not a condition even in ijtihadi matters; laypeople's opinions suffice.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
What is the Hanafi response to al-Baqillani? Why does the author compare laypeople to cattle?
The response: 'They are like cattle, and they must follow the mujtahids.' Their opposition is not considered.
5
Apply the Rule
In a current debate among Hanafi jurists, a layperson disagrees. Does their disagreement prevent consensus?
No, because in ijtihadi matters, only mujtahids are counted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجتهد
وأهل الإجماع من كان مجتهدا صالحا إلا فيما يستغني فيه عن الاجتهاد ليس فيه هوى ولا فسق صفة لقوله: "مجتهدا" كأنه قال: أهل الإجماع من كان مجتهدا صالحا إلا فيما يستغني عن الرأي، فإنه لا يشترط فيه أهل الاجتهاد، بل لا بد فيه من اتفاق الكل من الخواص والعوام حتى لو خالف واحد منهم لم يكن إجماعًا كنقل القرآن وأعداد الركعات ومقادير الزكاة، واستقراض الخبز، والاستحمام، وقال أبو بكر الباقلاني إن الاجتهاد ليس بشرط في المسائل الاجتهادية أيضًا، ويكفي قول العوام في انعقاد الإجماع، والجواب أنهم كالأنعام، وعليهم أن يقلدوا المجتهدين، ولا يعتبر خلافهم فيما يجب عليهم من التقليد.
وكونه من الصحابة هم أو من العترة لا يشترط يعني قال بعضهم: لا إجماع إلا للصحابة؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام مدحهم وأثنى عليهم الخير، فهم الأصول في علم الشريعة، وفيه أحاديث كثيرة منها: قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "خير القرون قرني، ثم الذين يلونهم ثم الذين يلونهم" فإن المراد بالقرن الثاني هو قرن الصحابة منه، هذا ما عليه المحدثون كما بسطه في فتح الباري، وأما ما ذكره بعض أعيان الزهلي في إزالة الخفاء أن المراد بالذين يلونهم قرن الشيخين وبالذين يلونهم قرن ذي النورين وأثبت به خلافة الخلفاء الثلاثة، فمخالف لجمهور المحدثين. كذا أفاده الأستاذ سلمه الله تعالى في ما علقه على إزالة الخفاء. [إشراق الأبصار: ۲۸]
Al-Mujtahid: And the people of consensus are whoever is a mujtahid, righteous, except in what is independent of ijtihad, without possessing desire or transgression. A description of his statement: 'mujtahid,' as if he said: The people of consensus are whoever is a mujtahid, righteous, except in what is independent of opinion, for in it ijtihad is not required; rather, the agreement of all, elite and commoners, is necessary, to the extent that if one of them opposes, it is not consensus, such as the transmission of the Qur'an, the numbers of rak'ahs, the amounts of zakat, borrowing bread, and bathing. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said that ijtihad is not a condition even in ijtihadi issues, and the statement of commoners suffices for the establishment of consensus. The response is that they are like cattle, and they must follow the mujtahids, and their opposition is not considered in what is obligatory upon them of taqlid.
And (the condition) that they be among the Companions or the prophetic family is not required, meaning some said: There is no consensus except for the Companions, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) praised them and commended them for good, and they are the foundations in the knowledge of the Shari'a. And there are many hadiths about this, including his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of generations is my generation, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' For the second generation is intended to be the generation of the Companions according to that, as the hadith scholars hold, as elaborated in Fath al-Bari. As for what some notable al-Zahli mentioned in Izaalat al-Khafa' that the intent of 'those who follow them' is the generation of the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and 'Umar) and 'those who follow them' is the generation of the possessor of the two lights ('Uthman), and he thereby established the caliphate of the three caliphs, this contradicts the majority of hadith scholars. Thus stated the teacher, may Allah protect him, in his commentary on Izaalat al-Khafa'. [Ishraq al-Absar: 28]
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage defines Ahl al-Ijma' (the people whose consensus is authoritative). The author, based on the matn, states that a person qualifies only if he is a mujtahid and righteous ('adil). Ijtihad is required in matters requiring opinion, but in definitive matters like the number of rak'ahs, even commoners must agree. The Baqillani dispute is addressed: laypeople are like cattle and cannot be part of consensus in ijtihadi matters. Then a second dispute: some restrict Ijma' to the Sahaba, but the author rejects this, quoting the hadith 'Khair al-Qurun' to show later generations are also authoritative. The author defends the majority view of hadith scholars against al-Zahli's interpretation.

Key Terms
أهل الإجماع (Ahl al-Ijma'): The people whose unanimous agreement constitutes authoritative consensus; they must be mujtahid and 'adil.
مجتهد (mujtahid): One who exerts effort to derive rulings from the primary sources; capable of ijtihad.
صالح (salih): Righteous, 'adil, free from major sin and persistence on minor sins.
عامي (ammi): A layperson, not a mujtahid; incapable of independent legal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, who qualifies to be part of an Ijma' in ijtihadi matters?
A) Any Muslim, regardless of knowledge
B) Only the Companions of the Prophet
C) A mujtahid who is righteous and free from desire and transgression
D) All scholars and commoners equally

Key Takeaway
In ijtihadi matters, only mujtahids who are righteous count for Ijma'; laypeople are not included.

Is Ijma' Limited to the Companions? The Hanafi Rejection
Dispute on restricting Ijma' to the Companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that Ijma' is not restricted to the Companions, explain the evidence from the hadith 'Khair al-Qurun', and evaluate the alternative interpretation of al-Zahli.

Core Idea
Ijma' is not limited to the Sahaba; later qualified mujtahids can also form binding consensus.

This passage addresses a second dispute: some scholars restrict Ijma' to the Companions of the Prophet. The author of Noor al-Anwar rejects this view, citing the famous hadith 'The best of generations is my generation, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' He argues that the hadith praises later generations as well, so their consensus is also authoritative. The passage also critiques a specific interpretation by al-Zahli that attempted to use the hadith to prove the caliphate of the first three caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Restricted View
Find the opinion that Ijma' is only for the Companions. What reason do they give?
They argue because the Prophet praised the Companions, and they are the foundations of Shari'a.
2
Locate the Hanafi Rejection
The author says 'is not required' (لا يشترط). What evidence does he provide against restricting Ijma' to the Sahaba?
He cites the hadith: 'Khair al-Qurun...'
3
Analyze the Hadith Interpretation
The hadith mentions three generations. What does the author say about the meaning of 'those who follow them'?
The author states that the second generation refers to the Companions (according to most hadith scholars), not the generation after the Companions.
4
Critique al-Zahli's View
What did al-Zahli claim? How does the author respond?
Al-Zahli claimed the hadith refers to the caliphates of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. The author says this contradicts the majority of hadith scholars.
5
Apply: Can Later Scholars Form Ijma'?
Based on the Hanafi position, can the consensus of Hanafi mujtahids in the 5th century AH be considered Ijma'?
Yes, because the Hanafis do not restrict Ijma' to the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Mujtahid: And the people of consensus are whoever is a mujtahid, righteous, except in what is independent of ijtihad, without possessing desire or transgression. Commentary
This passage defines Ahl al-Ijma' (the people whose consensus is authoritative). The author, based on the matn, states that a person qualifies only if he is a mujtahid and righteous ('adil). Key Terms
الصحابة (al-Sahaba): The Companions of the Prophet, those who met him believing and died as Muslims.
القرن (al-Qarn): A generation; in the hadith, it refers to a century or a group of people living at the same time.
العترة (al-`Itra): The prophetic family (Ahl al-Bayt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, is Ijma' restricted to the Companions?
A) Yes, only the Companions can form Ijma'.
B) No, because the hadith 'Khair al-Qurun' praises later generations.
C) Yes, because the Companions are the best generation.
D) No, but only the Prophet's family can form Ijma'.

Key Takeaway
Ijma' is not limited to the Companions; the consensus of qualified mujtahids from any generation is authoritative.

The Role of Commoners in Ijma': Necessary vs. Ijtihadi Matters
Opening discussion of Ijma‘ and commoners' participation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (ḍarūrī) and ijtihādī matters in Ijma‘ and apply the Hanafi rule regarding the role of commoners.

Core Idea
In Ijma‘, commoners count only in necessary matters; in ijtihādī matters, only mujtahids matter.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi distinction between matters known by necessity (like the number of rak‘ahs) and matters requiring legal reasoning (like contract details). The passage clarifies that in necessary matters, agreement of all Muslims – elite and commoners – is required; in ijtihādī matters, only the consensus of qualified mujtahids counts. This refutes al-Bāqillānī’s claim that commoners participate even in ijtihādī Ijma‘.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of matters
The text distinguishes between matters that do not require ijtihad (e.g., Quran, rak‘ahs, zakat amounts) and matters that do (e.g., details of marriage, divorce, sales). In the first category, everyone’s agreement is needed; in the second, only mujtahids.
This categorization is key to the entire discussion.
2
Understand the Hanafi rule for necessary matters
For matters like the number of prayers or fasting obligations – which are transmitted and known by necessity – the agreement of all Muslims, including laymen, is required. If even one layman opposes, it is not Ijma‘, and that dissenter is considered a disbeliever.
This shows that Ijma‘ in necessary matters is as binding as the text itself.
3
Understand the Hanafi rule for ijtihādī matters
In matters requiring ijtihad (e.g., contract details), only the agreement of those qualified to exercise ijtihad counts. Laymen are not qualified because they lack the tools (āla) for derivation; their opinion counts as nothing.
Hanafis compare laymen to children or insane persons in this context.
4
Analyze the refutation of al-Bāqillānī
Al-Bāqillānī argued that even in ijtihādī matters, the agreement of commoners is needed and their opinion counts. The Hanafi response: laymen are like cattle (al-an‘ām); they must follow (taqlīd) the mujtahids, and any alleged opposition from them in ijtihādī matters is irrelevant.
The hadith of ‘following the truth’ does not apply because laymen cannot independently discern truth in these matters.
5
Note the consequence of lay opposition
If a layman opposes a matter that is known by necessity (e.g., that prayer is obligatory), he is considered a disbeliever. But if he opposes an ijtihādī matter that is agreed upon by all mujtahids, he is not necessarily a disbeliever, but his opposition is legally irrelevant.
This distinction has practical implications for takfīr.
