(Sanbali) 'And probability' and what appeared in the margins of al-Da'ir in place of 'probability' the word 'doubt' is a slip of the pen; for there is no consensus that gives doubt, but the lowest rank of consensus is like a single report, giving probability not certainty, and necessitates action. (Qamar) 'Explicitly etc.' its definitiveness is due to consensus, since the opposition of its denier is not considered. (Fath al-Ghaffar: 355) 'On the caliphate etc.' as stated by Ibn al-Humam in al-Tahrir. (Qamar) 'On the caliphate' because the Muhajirun and Ansar agreed on the caliphate of Abu Bakr before burying the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) by silent consensus, like the consensus of the Companions on fighting the refusers of Zakat, for most of the Companions spoke [in favor] and some remained silent accepting. (Qamar) 'And its denier is not deemed a disbeliever' but rather its denier is deemed an innovator due to the existence of the opposition of al-Shafi'i, as has passed. Just as the implication of a general term is definitive according to us, but its denier is not deemed a disbeliever due to the opposition of al-Shafi'i, for the implication of the general term is probabilistic according to him. (Qamar)
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi usul text Noor al-Anwar discusses the nature and authority of Ijma (consensus). The author first explains that consensus can be established through analogy (qiyas), citing the example of the prohibition of riba in rice based on analogy to the six classical items, and also from explicit Quranic texts as a cause (dai). Then he addresses the transmission of consensus: if transmitted through the continuous consensus of each generation (tawatur), it gives certain knowledge and demands action; if transmitted through individual reports (ahad), it demands action but does not provide certainty. This is likened to mutawatir vs. ahad hadith. The text then delves into a dispute: some argue that if a ruling is already established by Quran and well-known Sunnah, consensus is unnecessary. The author refutes this by noting that consensus can still serve as reinforcement (ta'kid). A note from al-Talwih dismisses the dispute as meaningless. The passage also contrasts with Imam al-Ghazali and some Hanafis who argue that a definitive matter transmitted through ahad does not necessitate action; the Hanafi position, supported by the author, affirms that it does. The second part of the passage categorizes the levels (maratib) of consensus itself, independent of its transmission. The strongest is the explicit consensus of the Companions, which is like a Quranic verse or mutawatir report—its denier is deemed a disbeliever (kafir). An example is the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate. The next level is silent consensus (ijma sukti), where some Companions voice an opinion and others remain silent; its denier is not considered a disbeliever but is considered misguided (mudallal). The passage further distinguishes consensus of later generations with or without prior disagreement. The Hanafi view on 'amm (general term) is also invoked: the denier of a definitive 'amm is not declared a disbeliever because of the opposing view of al-Shafi'i, who considers 'amm probabilistic. This shows the Hanafi principle that takfir requires consensus that is explicit and uninterrupted.

Key Terms
الإجماع السكوتي (al-Ijma' al-Sukuti): Silent consensus: where some mujtahids express an opinion and others remain silent, indicating agreement. This does not reach the level of certainty.
العام (al-'Amm): A general term that applies to all individuals of its category; in Hanafi usul, its implication is definitive (qat'i) until a specification (takhsis) is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a person who denies a silent consensus of the Sahaba?
A) Kafir (disbeliever)
B) Fasiq (sinner)
C) Mudallal (misguided) but not kafir
D) There is no ruling

Key Takeaway
Explicit Sahaba consensus rises to the level of certain knowledge; silent consensus is probabilistic, so its denier is not excommunicated.

The Four Ranks of Ijma' in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the hierarchy of ijma' ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four ranks of ijma', state their epistemic weight, and apply the ruling on takfir for each rank.

Core Idea
Ijma' has four ranks; explicit consensus equals Quran, silent consensus is qat'i but no takfir.

Consensus (ijma') is a primary source of Islamic law. However, not all consensus carries the same weight. The Hanafi school distinguishes four ranks of ijma' based on the era and the manner of agreement. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for evaluating the binding force of a claimed consensus and the consequences of denying it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish transmission from inherent strength
Recognize that the passage distinguishes between the strength of the consensus itself (inherent) and the strength of its transmission. The hierarchy focuses on the former.
2
Identify the strongest rank
The first rank is explicit consensus of the Companions (ijma' nass). This is equivalent to the Quran and mutawatir hadith. Denying it results in disbelief (kufr).
Example: consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate.
3
Analyze silent consensus
The second rank is silent consensus (ijma' sukuti). It is definitive (qat'i) but its denier is not declared a disbeliever. Understand the Hanafi rationale for this distinction.
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'is) consider it non-binding; this is a key ikhtilaf.
4
Compare the lower two ranks
The third rank (later consensus without prior disagreement) provides tranquility (tuma'ninah) like the famous hadith. The fourth rank (consensus after prior disagreement) is like a solitary hadith, requiring action but not knowledge.
Note the difference in epistemic certainty: certainty (yaqin) vs. tranquility vs. action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإجماع
بيان مراتب أهل الإجماع
قبل الظهر، وتحريم نكاح الأخت في عدّة الأخت، وتوكيد المهر بالخلوة الصحيحة، ولم يتعرّض لتمثيله بالحديث المشهور؛ إذ لا فرق بينه وبين المتواتر إلا عدم اشتهاره في أي الخبر المشهور قرن الصحابة منه، وهذا لم يستقم ههنا؛ لأن الإجماع لم يكن في زمن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنما يكون في زمن الصحابة ، فبعده ليس إلا آحاد أو متواتر.
ثم هو على مراتب أي الإجماع في نفسه مع قطع النظر عن نقله له مراتب في القوة، والضعف واليقين والظن.
فالأقوى إجماع الصحابة نَصًّا مثل أن يقولوا جميعًا : أجمعنا على كذا، فإنه مثل الآية والخبر المتواتر حتى يكفّر جاحده، ومنه الإجماع على خلافة أبي بكر أي في إفادة اليقين.
ثم الذي نَصَّ البعض وسكت الباقون من الصحابة ، وهو المسمّى بالإجماع السكوتي، ولا يكفّر جاحده.
وإن كان من الأدلة القطعية.
ثم إجماع من بعدهم، أي بعد الصحابة هم من أهل كل عصر على حكم لم يظهر فيه خلاف من سبقهم من الصحابة فهو بمنزلة الخبر المشهور يفيد الطمأنينة دون اليقين.
ثم إجماعهم على قول سبق فيه مخالف، يعني اختلفوا أولاً على قولين، ثم أجمع من بعدهم على قول واحد، فهذا دون الكل، فهو بمنزلة خبر الواحد يوجب العمل دون العلم، ويكون مقدمًا على القياس كخبر الواحد.
والأمة إذا اختلفوا في مسألة في أيّ عصر كان على أقوال كان إجماعًا منهم على أن ما عداها باطل ولا يجوز لمن بعدهم إحداث قول آخر كما في الحامل المتوفى عنها زوجها، قيل: تعتد بعدة الحامل وقيل بأبعد الأجلين ولا يجوز أن تعتدّ بعدة الوفاة إذا لم تكن أبعد الأجلين. وقيل: هذا في الصحابة خاصةً، أي بطلان القول الثالث في الصحابة فقط؛ نص عليه.
Chapter of Consensus. Explanation of the ranks of the people of consensus. Before Zuhr, and prohibition of marrying a sister during the iddah of her sister, and confirmation of mahr by valid seclusion, and he did not address exemplifying it with the famous hadith; as there is no difference between it and the mutawatir except the lack of its fame in any century of the Companions, and this is not correct here because consensus did not occur in the time of the Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, but rather occurs in the time of the Companions, so thereafter there is only ahad or mutawatir. Then it has ranks, i.e., consensus itself, regardless of its transmission, has ranks in strength, weakness, certainty, and conjecture. The strongest is the explicit consensus of the Companions, such as all of them saying: We have agreed upon such-and-such, for it is like the verse and mutawatir hadith, to the extent that its denier is deemed a disbeliever. Included in this is the consensus on the caliphate of Abu Bakr, i.e., in providing certainty. Then that which some Companions explicitly stated and others remained silent, which is called silent consensus (ijma' sukuti), and its denier is not deemed a disbeliever. Even though it is from the definitive proofs. Then the consensus of those after them, i.e., after the Companions, who are the people of every era upon a ruling in which no disagreement appeared from the preceding Companions, it is like the famous hadith, providing tranquility without certainty. Then their consensus upon a statement in which a dissenter preceded, meaning they first disagreed on two statements, then later generations agreed on one statement—this is the lowest of all; it is like a solitary hadith, necessitating action without knowledge, and it is given precedence over analogy like a solitary hadith. And if the ummah disagrees on a matter in any era upon several statements, it is a consensus that what is other than those is invalid, and it is not permissible for those after them to introduce another statement, as in the case of a pregnant woman whose husband dies: it is said she observes the iddah of a pregnant woman, and it is said she observes the longer of the two periods, and it is not permissible for her to observe the iddah of widowhood if it is not the longer. And it is said: this applies specifically to the Companions, i.e., the invalidity of a third statement is only regarding the Companions; this is stated explicitly.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on consensus (ijma') explains the hierarchical ranks of consensus in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by stating that consensus itself, regardless of its transmission, has varying degrees of strength and weakness, certainty and conjecture. The strongest rank is the explicit consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahabah nassan), where all Companions verbally declare agreement on a ruling. This type of consensus is equal to the Quran and mutawatir hadith in providing certainty, to the extent that its denier is declared a disbeliever (yukaffar jahiduhu). An example is the consensus on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. The second rank is the 'silent consensus' (ijma' sukuti), where some Companions express an opinion and others remain silent, indicating tacit agreement. This type does not elevate its denier to disbelief, though it is still considered a definitive proof (qat'i) in terms of its authority. The third rank is the consensus of later generations (after the Companions) on a ruling where no disagreement is known from the Companions; this is compared to the mashhur hadith, providing tranquility (tuma'ninah) but not absolute certainty. The fourth and weakest rank is consensus on a ruling where there was prior disagreement (say, two opinions among the Companions), and later generations agree on one. This is like a khabar wahid, necessitating action but not knowledge, and it takes precedence over qiyas (analogy). Finally, the passage addresses the situation where the ummah (or specifically the Companions) disagree on multiple opinions in a given era: the consensus is that only those opinions are valid and no third opinion may be introduced. The example given is the pregnant widow's iddah: some say she observes the iddah of pregnancy (until delivery), others say she observes the longer of the two periods (pregnancy or four months and ten days), but it is not allowed to adopt only the widowhood iddah if it is shorter. Some scholars restrict this prohibition of introducing a third opinion to the era of the Companions only.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة نصاً (ijmā' al-ṣaḥābah naṣṣan): Explicit consensus where all Companions verbally agree on a ruling.
الإجماع السكوتي (al-ijmā' al-sukūtī): Consensus where some Companions state an opinion and others remain silent, indicating approval.
الخبر المشهور (al-khabar al-mashhūr): Hadith transmitted by at least three in every generation, not reaching tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which rank of ijmā' is compared to the 'mashhūr hadith' in terms of providing tranquility without certainty?
A) Explicit consensus of the Companions
B) Silent consensus of the Companions
C) Consensus of later generations on a ruling with no prior disagreement from the Companions
D) Consensus on a ruling after prior disagreement

Key Takeaway
Ijma' has four ranks: explicit consensus (like Quran), silent consensus (qat'i, no takfir), later consensus (tuma'ninah), and consensus after prior disagreement (like ahad, action without knowledge).

The Rule of Third Opinions After Disagreement in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the rule that no third opinion is allowed after the ummah disagrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that after the ummah disagrees on a matter, no new opinion may be introduced, and evaluate whether this applies only to the Companions.

Core Idea
When the ummah disagrees on a ruling, the existing opinions are validated and no third opinion is allowed; this itself is an ijma' on the limited scope.

The passage concludes with an important principle: when the ummah (or specifically the Companions) disagrees on a matter, the disagreement itself constitutes a form of ijma' that the existing opinions are the only valid ones. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application to the example of the pregnant widow's iddah, as well as the dispute over its temporal scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario of disagreement
The passage describes a situation where the ummah in any era disagrees on a matter, arriving at multiple valid opinions (e.g., two opinions). The very existence of this disagreement becomes a consensus that only these opinions are valid.
2
Analyze the pregnant widow example
The example given: a pregnant widow's iddah. Some say she observes the iddah of pregnancy (until delivery), others say the longer of the two periods (pregnancy or 4 months 10 days). It is not permissible to observe only the widowhood iddah if it is shorter than the other option. This illustrates that a third opinion (the shorter iddah) is invalid.
The two valid opinions are both from Companions or later scholars; the third is rejected.
3
Identify the ikhtilaf on scope
Some scholars (as reported in the text) state that this prohibition of a third opinion applies only to the Companions' era, not to later generations. Others hold it applies universally.
The text says 'وقيل: هذا في الصحابة خاصةً' indicating a minority view.
4
Evaluate the implications
If the rule is restricted to the Companions, then later scholars can introduce new opinions on matters where the Companions disagreed. If universal, then the original disagreement is fixed and no new opinion can be added.
This affects the scope of ijtihad and the development of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Ijma — Consensus

12 min
Ranks of Ijma and the Invalidity of a Third Opinion
Usul rule on ranks of ijma and invalidity of third opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the epistemic ranks of ijma and explain why a third opinion is invalid when two positions exist, according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Two opinions form a composite consensus; any third is automatically invalid.

This lesson unpacks a crucial Hanafi usul doctrine: the notion of composite consensus (al-ijmāʿ al-murakkab). When earlier mujtahids differ on exactly two positions, those two positions together constitute a consensus that no other view is valid. This principle, which the text illustrates with the pregnant widow's iddah and the annulment due to defects, is the foundation for the traditional confinement of acceptable legal schools to four.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major categories of later ijma
The passage divides ijma after the Companions into two kinds: (a) consensus on a matter with no prior disagreement, which is like a well-known report (khabar mashhur) giving reassurance but not certainty; (b) consensus after prior disagreement, which is like a solitary report (khabar wahid), requiring action but not knowledge, and takes precedence over qiyas.
Epistemic weight differs: first category yields tuma'nina, second yields only zann.
2
Extract the general rule about third opinions
The passage states: 'the Ummah if they differ in a matter in any era on opinions, it is a consensus from them that what is other than them is invalid, and it is not permissible for those after them to initiate another opinion.' The rationale is that the truth must lie within the existing opinions; otherwise ignorance or concealment would follow.
This rule applies regardless of whether the differing scholars are Companions or later, according to the preferred view.
3
Analyze the dispute: Companions only or all generations?
Some Hanafis restrict the invalidity of a third opinion to the Companions due to their special status. The preferred view (championed by al-Qamar in the commentary) extends it to all eras, forming the basis for composite consensus and the confinement to four madhhabs. The practical difference: if restricted, later mujtahids could introduce new opinions; if not, the four madhhabs are exhaustive.
The text quotes Quran 4:115 'following other than the path of the believers' as an underlying proof.
4
Work through the examples: pregnant widow and annulment defects
For the pregnant widow, two opinions exist: iddah by delivery or by the longer period. A third opinion (iddah of death if shorter) is invalid. For annulment due to defects, two extreme opinions exist (always annul or never annul); the middle ground (annul in some cases) has no proponent – this is 'adam al-qā'il bi al-faṣl, a subtype of composite consensus.
These examples show how the rule operates in practice and leads to closure of further ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإجماع
بيان مراتب أهل الإجماع
وإن كان من الأدلة القطعية.
ثم إجماع من بعدهم، أي بعد الصحابة هم من أهل كل عصر على حكم لم يظهر فيه خلاف من سبقهم من الصحابة منه فهو بمنزلة الخبر المشهور يفيد الطمأنينة دون اليقين. ثم إجماعهم على قول سبق فيه مخالف، يعني اختلفوا أولاً على قولين، ثم أجمع من بعدهم على قول واحد، فهذا دون الكل، فهو بمنزلة خبر الواحد يوجب العمل دون العلم، ويكون مقدمًا على القياس كخبر الواحد.
والأمة إذا اختلفوا في مسألة في أيّ عصر كان على أقوال كان إجماعًا منهم على أن ما عداها باطل ولا يجوز لمن بعدهم إحداث قول آخر كما في الحامل المتوفى عنها زوجها، قيل: تعتد بعدة الحامل وقيل بأبعد الأجلين ولا يجوز أن تعتدّ بعدة الوفاة إذا لم تكن أبعد الأجلين. وقيل: هذا في الصحابة لله، خاصةً، أي بطلان القول الثالث في الصحابة له فقط؛ نص عليه.
وإن كان إلخ أي وإن كان هذا الإجماع في الأصل من الأدلة القطعية، قال الشارح في "المنهية": عد الإجماع السكوتي ههنا من الأدلة القطعية، وقال فيما سبق "إنه لا يفيد القطع " ؛ لأنه أراد ثمه قطعية موجبة للتكفير فلا تدافع. (القمر) يفيد الطمأنينة لأن هذا الإجماع مختلف فيه على ما قد مرّ، فإن البعض قالوا إنه لا إجماع إلا بالصحابة؛ فأورث شبهة سقط بها اليقين، وهو يوجب العمل. (القمر) إذا اختلفوا هذا عند الأكثر في "التفسير" الإمام محمد والشافعي حملها في رسالته، وخصه بعض الحنفية بالصحابة كما قاله المصنف بقوله: وقيل هذا إلخ، أي قالوا إذا اختلفوا الصحابة على قولين لم يجز إحداث ثالث وأما إذا اختلف من بعدهم فيجوز إحداث ثالث ولكن لا يظهر فارق (السنبلي)
تعتد بعدّة الحامل أي وضع الحمل، وهذا هو قول ابن مسعود، واختاره إمامنا الأعظم له. (القمر) بعدة الحامل إلخ سواء كان أبعد الأجلين أو أقربهما، وقوله : "بأبعد الأجلين" أي سواء كان هو عدّة الحامل أو عدة الوفات. (السنبلي) بعدة الحامل إلخ أي بالوضع كما عن ابن مسعود وأبي هريرة لها قوله، وقيل: بأبعد الأجلين أي من الوضع والأشهر كما عن أمير المؤمنين عليه وابن عباس ما فيما يقال، فاتفق الكل على نفى الأشهر، فلا يقال بالأشهر فقط، والأرفع ما اتفقا عليه في المسألة. (السنبلي) بأبعد الأجلين أي ما كان أبعد من عدة الوفاة ووضع الحمل فهو عدتها . (القمر) هذا في الصحابة منه خاصة لتقدّم الصحابة في الاجتهاد وعلمهم بموارد النصوص، وبركة صحبة النبي (القمر)
فإنهم إن اختلفوا على قولين كان إجماعًا على بطلان القول الثالث دون سائر الأمة، ولكن الحق أن بطلان القول الثالث مطلق يجري في اختلاف كل عصر، وهذا يسمّى إجماعًا مركبا؛ لأنه نشأ من اختلاف قولين وهو أقسام قسم منها يسمى بعدم القائل بالفصل، وقد بينها صاحب "التوضيح" بما لا يتصور المزيد عليه، وعندي أن هذا المنشأ لانحصار المذاهب في الأربعة وبطلان الخامس المستحدث، ولكن يرد عليه أنه إن أريد بالاختلاف الاختلاف مشافهةً في زمان واحد فينبغي يجري في اختلاف إلخ أي ليس فيه تخصيص بالصحابة، فإن المجتهدين إذا اختلفوا على أقوال، فوقع الاتفاق على القدر المشترك بين تلك الأقوال، وعلى أن الحق ليس بخارج من هذه الأقوال، وإلا يلزم الجهل أو كتمان الحق، فالقول الخارج يكون غير سبيل المؤمنين، فيصير باطلا. (القمر)
بعدم القائل بالفصل :إلخ كالتفصيل في الفسخ بالعيوب المرض والجذام والجنون في أيهما كانت، والجب والعنة في الزوج، والرتق والقرن في الزوجة، فقيل: لا يوجب الفسخ أصلاً، وقيل: نعم، يوجب الفسخ في الكل، فالتفصيل لم يقل به أحد، فهو قول ثالث ويعبر عن هذا بعدم القائل بالفصل. (السنبلي) صاحب "التوضيح" إلخ محمل بيانه أن القولين إن كانا يشتركان في أمر هو في الحقيقة واحد، وهو من الأحكام الشرعية فحينئذٍ يكون القول الثالث مستلزما لإبطال الإجماع، وإلا فلا، وعند ذلك نقول: إن المختلف فيه إما حكم متعلق بمحل واحد أو حكم متعلق بأكثر من محل واحد، أما الأول فكما في الخارج من غير السبيلين، فإن الواجب هو التطهير بالإجماع، وهو الوضوء عندنا، وغسل المخرج عند الشافعي له، فالقول بأن لا شيء من التطهير بواجب خلاف الإجماع، وأما الثاني فإما أن يكون الثابت عند البعض الوجود في صورة مع العدم في الأخرى، وعند البعض عكس ذلك كمسألة الخروج من غير السبيلين ومس المرأة، فالقول بانتقاض كل منهما مخالف لقول أبي حنيفة له في مسألة المس، ولقول الشافعي له في مسألة الخروج، وإما أن يكون الثابت عند البعض الوجود في الصورتين وعند البعض العدم في الصورتين، ويسمى هذا عدم القائل بالفصل، والإجماع المركب أعم منه، نظيره أنه ليس للأب والجد ولاية إجبار البالغة على النكاح عندنا، وعند الشافعي ليه لكل واحد منهما ولاية الإجبار، فالقول بولاية الأب دون الجد خلاف الإجماع، إلى آخر ما فصل في "التوضيح". (القمر) هذا الأصل: أي قول المصنف والأمة إذا اختلفوا إلخ. (القمر)
لانحصار المذاهب واعلم أن المقرّر عند أهل السنة والجماعة أن المذاهب الأربع حق بمعنى أنها معمولة مشافهة وإذا لم يكن الاختلاف بالمشافهة فهو باطل. (المحشي)
Chapter of Consensus. Explanation of the ranks of the people of consensus. And if it is from the decisive proofs. Then consensus of those after them, i.e., after the Companions, they are the people of every era on a ruling in which no disagreement from those before them among the Companions has appeared, it is like the well-known report, giving reassurance without certainty. Then their consensus on a statement in which a dissenter preceded, meaning they first disagreed on two opinions, then those after them agreed on one opinion, this is below all, it is like a solitary report, requiring action but not knowledge, and it is prioritized over analogy like a solitary report. Or two opinions. And the Ummah if they differ in a matter in any era on opinions, it is a consensus from them that what is other than them is invalid, and it is not permissible for those after them to initiate another opinion, as in the case of a pregnant woman whose husband has died: it was said she observes the iddah of a pregnant woman, and it was said [she observes] the longer of the two periods, and it is not permissible for her to observe the iddah of death if it is not the longer of the two periods. And it was said: this is [only] for the Companions, specifically, i.e., the invalidity of the third opinion applies only to the Companions; it is explicitly stated. And if it is etc. meaning even if this consensus originally is from the decisive proofs. The commentator said in al-Munyat: the silent consensus is counted here among the decisive proofs, and he said earlier that it does not give certainty; because he intended there proofs requiring takfir, so there is no contradiction. (al-Qamar) It gives reassurance because this consensus is disputed as previously mentioned, for some said there is no consensus except with the Companions; so it gave a doubt by which certainty dropped, and it requires action. (al-Qamar) When they differed, this is according to most in al-Tafsir: Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i carried it in his Risala, and some Hanafis restricted it to the Companions as the author said by his saying: 'and it was said this etc.', i.e., they said when the Companions differ on two opinions, it is not permissible to initiate a third, but if those after them differ, then initiating a third is permissible, but no distinction appears. (al-Sanabali) She observes iddah of a pregnant woman, i.e., delivery. This is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud, and our great Imam chose it. (al-Qamar) By iddah of the pregnant woman etc., whether it is the longer or shorter of the two periods, and his saying: 'by the longer of the two periods' meaning whether it is the iddah of the pregnant woman or the iddah of death. (al-Sanabali) By iddah of the pregnant woman etc., i.e., by delivery as reported from Ibn Mas'ud and Abu Hurayra, and it was said: by the longer of the two periods, i.e., between delivery and months, as reported from the Commander of the Faithful and Ibn Abbas, they say, so all agreed on the negation of months, so it is not said by months only, and the most authoritative is what they both agreed upon in the matter. (al-Sanabali) By the longer of the two periods, i.e., whichever is longer between the iddah of death and delivery of the pregnancy is her iddah. (al-Qamar) This is specifically for the Companions due to the precedence of the Companions in ijtihad and their knowledge of the sources of texts, and the blessing of the Prophet's company. (al-Qamar) For if they differ on two opinions, it is a consensus on the invalidity of the third opinion only for the rest of the Ummah, but the truth is that the invalidity of the third opinion applies absolutely in the difference of every era, and this is called composite consensus; because it arises from the difference of two opinions, and it has types, one of which is called 'the absence of a proponent of distinction', and the author of al-Tawdih explained it in a manner beyond which nothing more can be imagined, and in my view this is the origin of the confinement of the schools to four and the invalidity of the fifth innovated one. But it is objected that if disagreement is intended as oral disagreement in a single time, then it should run in the difference of every era etc., i.e., there is no specification to the Companions, for if the mujtahids differ on opinions, then there is agreement on the common factor among those opinions, and that the truth is not outside these opinions; otherwise ignorance or concealment of truth would be necessary, so the external opinion would be other than the path of the believers, thus becoming invalid. (al-Qamar) The absence of a proponent of distinction: such as the detail in annulment due to defects: leprosy, vitiligo, and insanity in whichever of them, and impotence and castration in the husband, and atresia and septate [vagina] in the wife. For it was said: it does not necessitate annulment at all, and it was said: yes, it necessitates annulment in all cases. So the detail was not said by anyone, so it is a third opinion, and this is called 'the absence of a proponent of distinction'. (al-Sanabali) The author of al-Tawdih etc., the interpretation clarifies that if the two opinions share in a matter that is in reality one, and it is from the legal rulings, then the third opinion would entail invalidating the consensus; otherwise not. And along these lines we say: the disputed matter is either a ruling related to one locus or a ruling related to more than one locus. As for the first, like exiting from other than the two passages: the obligatory is purification by consensus, which is ablution with us, and washing the exit with al-Shafi'i. So the saying that no purification is obligatory contradicts consensus. As for the second, either what is established for some is existence in one situation with non-existence in the other, and for some the opposite, like the issue of exiting from other than the two passages and touching a woman. So the saying that each of them breaks ablution contradicts the saying of Abu Hanifa on the issue of touching, and the saying of al-Shafi'i on the issue of exiting. Or what is established for some is existence in both situations, and for some non-existence in both, and this is called 'the absence of a proponent of distinction', and composite consensus is more general than it. An example: according to us, the father and grandfather do not have the guardianship to compel an adult woman to marry; according to al-Shafi'i, each of them has the guardianship of compulsion. So the saying of a father's guardianship without the grandfather contradicts consensus. And so on until what was detailed in al-Tawdih. (al-Qamar) This is the principle: i.e., the author's saying 'and the Ummah if they differ etc.' (al-Qamar) For the confinement of the schools, and know that the established view among Ahl al-Sunna is that the four schools are true in the sense that they are acted upon orally, and if the difference is not oral, it is invalid. (al-Muhashi)
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Ijma explains the ranks of consensus and the ruling on introducing a third opinion when earlier scholars had two opinions. The text distinguishes between consensus of generations after the Companions: if no prior disagreement exists, it resembles a well-known report (khabar mashhur) giving reassurance (tuma'nina) but not certainty (yaqin). If there was prior disagreement, it is like a solitary report (khabar wahid), requiring action but not knowledge, and takes precedence over qiyas. It then discusses a general rule: when the Ummah differs on a matter (whether Companions or later), the existence of two opinions constitutes a consensus that any other view is invalid, and no one may invent a new opinion. The example given is the pregnant widow: two opinions exist—her iddah is by delivery (Ibn Mas'ud, Abu Hanifa) or by the longer period (Ali, Ibn Abbas). A third opinion (only iddah of death if shorter) is invalid. The text then records a disagreement: some say this invalidity of a third opinion applies only to the Companions due to their special status; others hold it applies generally. The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sanabali expands on these points. Finally, the concept of 'composite consensus' (ijma' murakkab) is introduced, with subcategories including 'absence of a proponent of distinction' (adam al-qā'il bi al-faṣl), where the two opinions are on opposite extremes and the middle position is not held by anyone. Examples from marriage annulment due to defects and from wudu rulings illustrate this. The passage also touches on the idea that the confinement to four madhhabs is based on such composite consensus.

Key Terms
الإجماع المركب (al-ijmāʿ al-murakkab): A consensus that arises from two (or more) existing opinions, such that any new opinion beyond them is invalid.
عدم القائل بالفصل (ʿadam al-qāʾil bi al-faṣl): A subtype of composite consensus where two extreme opinions exist and no scholar has adopted the middle ground that distinguishes between them.
الخبر المشهور (al-khabar al-mashhūr): A hadith transmitted by at least three chains in every generation but not reaching mutawatir; it yields reassurance (ṭuma'nīna) but not certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the case of the pregnant widow, which of the following is the invalid third opinion?
A) Iddah by delivery
B) Iddah by the longer of the two periods
C) Iddah by death if it is shorter than the longer period
D) Iddah by four months and ten days

Key Takeaway
Composite consensus (al-ijma' al-murakkab) binds later scholars to the two existing opinions; any third opinion is invalid.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
8 min
Compound Consensus: Definition and Application
Defining compound consensus and its application to madhhab confinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define compound consensus (ijma' murakkab), distinguish its sub-type 'adam al-qa'il bi al-fasl, and apply the condition of mushafaha to evaluate the validity of a third opinion.

Core Idea
When scholars only hold two opinions, any third opinion is automatically invalid.

In this passage, we explore a subtle form of consensus that arises not from unanimous agreement on a single ruling, but from the shared rejection of a third possibility. This concept, known as ijma' murakkab, is foundational for understanding how the Hanafi school treats differences of opinion and why the four madhhabs are considered sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central definition
Look for the statement that when scholars differ on two opinions, it is consensus on the invalidity of a third opinion. Note that this is called 'compound consensus' because it arises from the combination of two views.
Do not confuse with simple consensus where all agree on one ruling.
2
Distinguish the sub-type: 'adam al-qa'il bi al-fasl
Find the explanation of 'absence of a proponent of the detail'. This occurs when two extreme opinions exist and no one advocates a middle ground; that middle ground is a third opinion invalidated by ijma'.
The example of annulment due to defects illustrates this.
3
Work through the annulment example
Consider the two opinions: (1) no annulment for any defect, (2) annulment for all defects. The detailed view (e.g., annulment only for certain defects) has no proponent, so it is a third opinion and invalid.
This shows how to identify a third opinion in practice.
4
Analyze the condition of mushafaha
The passage argues that valid consensus on a new opinion requires oral exchange (mushafaha) among contemporary scholars. Without it, later imams' views are not considered new for consensus purposes.
This condition is crucial for understanding the confinement of madhhabs.
5
Evaluate the dispute on later imams
An objection is raised: why disregard opinions of Shafi'i and Ahmad? The Hanafi response is that they were not contemporaneous with Abu Hanifa; their views derive from earlier sources or follow other mujtahids. Thus, no new consensus-breaking opinion emerges.
The outcome is that only four madhhabs are authoritative.

باب الإجماع
بيان مراتب أهل الإجماع فإنهم إن اختلفوا على قولين كان إجماعًا على بطلان القول الثالث دون سائر الأمة، ولكن الحق أن بطلان القول الثالث مطلق يجري في اختلاف كل عصر، وهذا يسمّى إجماعًا مركبا؛ لأنه نشأ من اختلاف قولين وهو أقسام قسم منها يسمى بعدم القائل بالفصل، وقد بينها صاحب "التوضيح" بما لا يتصور المزيد عليه، وعندي أن هذا المنشأ لانحصار المذاهب في الأربعة وبطلان الخامس المستحدث، ولكن يرد الأصل هو بعدم القائل بالفصل عليه أنه إن أريد بالاختلاف الاختلاف مشافهةً في زمان واحد فينبغي يجري في اختلاف إلخ أي ليس فيه تخصيص بالصحابة، فإن المجتهدين إذا اختلفوا على أقوال، فوقع الاتفاق على القدر المشترك بين تلك الأقوال، وعلى أن الحق ليس بخارج من هذه الأقوال، وإلا يلزم الجهل أو كتمان الحق، فالقول الخارج يكون غير سبيل المؤمنين، فيصير باطلا. (القمر) بعدم القائل بالفصل :إلخ كالتفصيل في الفسخ بالعيوب المرض والجذام والجنون في أيهما كانت، والجب والعنة في الزوج، والرتق والقرن في الزوجة، فقيل: لا يوجب الفسخ أصلاً، وقيل: نعم، يوجب الفسخ في الكل، فالتفصيل لم يقل به أحد، فهو قول ثالث ويعبر عن هذا بعدم القائل بالفصل. (السنبلي) صاحب "التوضيح" إلخ محمل بيانه أن القولين إن كانا يشتركان في أمر هو في الحقيقة واحد، وهو من الأحكام الشرعية فحينئذٍ يكون القول الثالث مستلزما لإبطال الإجماع، وإلا فلا، وعند ذلك نقول: إن المختلف فيه إما حكم متعلق بمحل واحد أو حكم متعلق بأكثر من محل واحد، أما الأول فكما في الخارج من غير السبيلين، فإن الواجب هو التطهير بالإجماع، وهو الوضوء عندنا، وغسل المخرج عند الشافعي له، فالقول بأن لا شيء من التطهير بواجب خلاف الإجماع، وأما الثاني فإما أن يكون الثابت عند البعض الوجود في صورة مع العدم في الأخرى، وعند البعض عكس ذلك كمسألة الخروج من غير السبيلين ومس المرأة، فالقول بانتقاض كل منهما مخالف لقول أبي حنيفة له في مسألة المس، ولقول الشافعي له في مسألة الخروج، وإما أن يكون الثابت عند البعض الوجود في الصورتين وعند البعض العدم في الصورتين، ويسمى هذا عدم القائل بالفصل، والإجماع المركب أعم منه، نظيره أنه ليس للأب والجد ولاية إجبار البالغة على النكاح عندنا، وعند الشافعي ليه لكل واحد منهما ولاية الإجبار، فالقول بولاية الأب دون الجد خلاف الإجماع، إلى آخر ما فصل في "التوضيح". (القمر) هذا الأصل: أي قول المصنف لله والأمة إذا اختلفوا إلخ. (القمر) لانحصار المذاهب واعلم أن المقرّر عند أهل السنة والجماعة أن المذاهب الأربع حق بمعنى أنها معمولة] مشافهة وإذا لم يكن الاختلاف بالمشافهة فهو باطل. (المحشي)

باب الإجماع
بيان مراتب أهل الإجماع
أن يكون مذهب الشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل حلها، باطلا، حين اختلف أبو حنيفة رحمه الله مع مالك رحمه في زمان واحد، وإن أريد بالاختلاف أعم من أن يكون في زمان واحد أم لا فكيف لا يعتبر اختلافنا كما اعتبر اختلاف الشافعي وأحمد ابن حنبل رحم؟ والجواب عنه صعب، وقد بالغت في تحقيقه في التفسير الأحمدي، وبذلت جهدي وطاقتي فيه، و لم يسبقني إلى مثله أحد، فطالعه إن شئت. مذهب الشافعي له إلخ : إذ لا مشافهة للشافعي له وأحمد بن حنبل له لأبي حنيفة رحمه. (القمر) باطلا لأنهما كانا بعد أبي حنيفة ومالك رحمه. (المحشي) في تحقيقه إلخ حتى أورد الجواب بقوله: الاختلاف المعتبر هو الذي في زمان واحد والشافعي له وغيره إذا قالوا قولاً إنما يقولون إذا جرى رأي أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمه مع أبي حنيفة له، أو كان اختلاف بين الصحابة ، فأخذ أبو حنيفة رحمه بقول صحابي رحمه، ومالك والشافعي رحمه بقول صحابي رحمه آخر، والأغلب أن شيئًا من المسائل لا يكون فيه أربع أقوال للأئمة الأربعة، بل يكون فيه قولان أو ثلاث وبعض من الأئمة يتبعون البعض، ولا يلزم أن يكون لكل من الأئمة الأربعة قول في كل وهكذا الحال في أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما وغيرهما، ولعل هذا أي اتحاد الزمان في غير المسائل القياسية، وأما المسائل القياسية فالمدار فيها على العلة، فمهما وجدها المجتهد مخالفًا للأول أو موافقًا له يعمل به والإنصاف أن انحصار المذاهب في الأربع واتباعهم فضل إلهي، وقبولية من عند الله تعالى لا مجال فيه للتوجيهات والأدلة. (القمر)
Chapter of Ijma' (Consensus). Explanation of the levels of the people of Ijma': if they differ on two opinions, it is consensus on the invalidity of a third opinion, not on the rest of the Ummah. But the truth is that the invalidity of the third opinion is absolute, applying to the difference of every era. This is called compound consensus because it arises from the difference of two opinions. It has divisions, one of which is called 'the absence of a proponent of the division'. The author of al-Tawdih has explained it in a way that no more can be imagined. In my view, this is the origin of the confinement of madhhabs to four and the invalidity of a newly invented fifth. But it is objected based on the principle [of] 'absence of a proponent of the division' that if by difference is meant oral difference in one time, then it should apply to difference etc. i.e., there is no specification of the Companions. For when the mujtahids differ on opinions, then agreement occurs on the common denominator among those opinions, and that the truth is not outside these opinions; otherwise, ignorance or concealment of truth would follow. So the outside opinion becomes the way of other than the believers, thus null. (al-Qamar) 'Absence of a proponent of the division': like the detail in annulment due to defects: illness, leprosy, insanity—which of them are present; impotence and eunuch in the husband; and retq and qarn in the wife. It is said: it does not necessitate annulment at all; and it is said: yes, it necessitates annulment in all. The detail is not held by anyone, so it is a third opinion, and this is called 'absence of a proponent of the division'. (al-Sanbali) The author of al-Tawdih etc. The meaning of his explanation is that if the two opinions share a matter that is in reality one, and it is from the legal rulings, then the third opinion entails nullification of consensus; otherwise not. Upon that we say: the matter of difference is either a ruling related to one locus or a ruling related to more than one locus. As for the first, like excretion from other than the two passages: purification is obligatory by consensus, which is wudu' according to us, and washing the exit according to al-Shafi'i. So the opinion that none of the purification is obligatory contradicts consensus. As for the second, either the existence in one form is established by some with non-existence in another form, and by others the opposite, like the issue of excretion from other than the two passages and touching a woman. So the opinion that both nullify contradicts Abu Hanifa's opinion on touching and al-Shafi'i's on excretion. Or existence in both forms is established by some and non-existence in both by others. This is called 'absence of a proponent of the division', and compound consensus is more general than it. An example: there is no right for father and grandfather to compel the adult woman into marriage according to us, while according to al-Shafi'i each of them has the right of compulsion. So the opinion of the father's right without the grandfather contradicts consensus. Up to the end of what is detailed in al-Tawdih. (al-Qamar) This is the principle: i.e., the author's statement 'for Allah and the Ummah if they differ etc.' (al-Qamar) Regarding the confinement of madhhabs: know that the established position of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is that the four madhhabs are true in the sense that they are practiced] orally; and if the difference is not oral, it is false. (al-Muhashi) Chapter of Ijma'. Explanation of levels of people of Ijma'. That the madhhab of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal be abrogator, invalid, when Abu Hanifa differed with Malik in one time. If by difference is meant more general than being in one time or not, then why is our difference not considered as the difference of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal was considered? The answer is difficult, and I have exerted myself in verifying it in al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi, and I have strived my utmost in it, and no one has preceded me to its like, so consult it if you wish. (al-Qamar) The madhhab of al-Shafi'i and others: because there is no oral exchange between al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal on one hand and Abu Hanifa on the other. (al-Muhashi) 'Invalid' because they were after Abu Hanifa and Malik. (al-Muhashi) In verifying it etc., until he presented the answer that the considered difference is that in one time, and al-Shafi'i and others only say a statement when the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concurred with Abu Hanifa, or there was difference among the Companions, so Abu Hanifa took the statement of one Companion, and Malik and al-Shafi'i took the statement of another Companion. Most issues do not have four statements from the four imams, but rather two or three, and some imams follow others. It is not necessary that each of the four imams have a statement on every issue. Similarly for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and others. Perhaps this [unity of time] is for non-qiyasi issues. As for qiyasi issues, the basis is the 'illa; whenever the mujtahid finds it, whether contradicting the prior or agreeing, he acts on it. The fair view is that the confinement of madhhabs to four and following them is a divine favor and acceptance from Allah, with no room for justifications and proofs. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of 'compound consensus' (ijma' murakkab), a key concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author explains that when scholars disagree on two opinions about a ruling, the fact that they agree on the invalidity of a third opinion constitutes a form of consensus. This is called compound consensus because it arises from the combination of two opposing views. He then distinguishes a sub-type called 'lack of a proponent of the detail' (adam al-qa'il bi al-fasl), where a detailed intermediate position has no advocate. Examples are given from annulment of marriage due to defects and from purification rules. The discussion then shifts to the confinement of madhhabs to four, addressing the objection that later imams like al-Shafi'i and Ahmad cannot be considered because they did not debate orally with Abu Hanifa. The commentator argues that the difference must be oral and contemporaneous; otherwise, later opinions are not valid. He also notes that most issues do not have four distinct opinions, and that the confinement to four is a divine blessing. This passage is from a hashiya on Nur al-Anwar, and the commentator's tone is both explanatory and defensive of the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الإجماع المركب (al-ijmāʿ al-murakkab): Compound consensus: the agreement of scholars on the common element between two conflicting opinions, resulting in the invalidity of any third opinion.
عدم القائل بالفصل (ʿadam al-qaʾil bi al-faṣl): Absence of a proponent of the detail: a situation where a detailed intermediate position between two extremes has no advocate, making any such detail a rejected third opinion.
المشافهة (al-mushāfaha): Oral exchange: direct verbal debate or communication between scholars in the same time period, considered necessary for valid consensus on a new opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'adam al-qa'il bi al-fasl' mean?
A) Absence of a proponent of the detail
B) Compound consensus
C) Oral exchange
D) Invalidity of a third opinion

Key Takeaway
Compound consensus is the agreement that when only two opinions exist, any third opinion is automatically invalid and outside the way of the believers.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
8 min
Contemporaneity in Ijma': Which Differences Count?
Explaining which differences among scholars are considered for ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that only contemporaneous differences of opinion prevent consensus (ijma') and distinguish between qiyasi and non-qiyasi issues in that context.

Core Idea
Only differences among contemporaries matter; later scholars' views do not break an earlier ijma'.

One of the key questions in usul al-fiqh is what qualifies as a valid difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) that can prevent the formation of consensus (ijma'). The Hanafi school holds a nuanced position: only disagreements among scholars who are contemporaries are relevant. Later scholars, such as al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who came after Abu Hanifa and Malik, do not have a direct difference that can break an already established consensus. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule and its application, including a special exception for qiyasi issues where the effective cause ('illa) is the guiding factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested point about ijma'
The passage addresses a potential objection: if a group of contemporaries (e.g., Abu Hanifa and Malik) agree on a ruling, can a later scholar (e.g., al-Shafi'i) disagree and break that consensus? The Hanafi position is that such later disagreement is not valid ikhtilaf for purposes of preventing ijma'.
The objection assumes that any disagreement, regardless of time, breaks consensus.
2
Understand the Hanafi condition of contemporaneity
The key condition is that the disagreeing scholars must have been alive at the same time. This is why the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Malik is relevant, but al-Shafi'i's later opinion is not—since al-Shafi'i did not directly address Abu Hanifa.
The text uses the term 'mushafahah' (direct address) to emphasize the lack of contemporaneity.
3
Analyze the argument for why al-Shafi'i's view does not count
The commentator (al-Qamar) explains that when later imams like al-Shafi'i express an opinion, they either follow the same Companions that Abu Hanifa followed, or they follow the opinions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Abu Hanifa's students). Thus, their view is not a new independent disagreement but a continuation. Also, most issues do not have four distinct opinions from the four imams; some imams simply follow others.
This reinforces that the later difference is not truly novel.
4
Note the distinction for qiyasi issues
The passage makes an exception for issues based on qiyas (analogy). For qiyasi issues, the ruling depends on the effective cause ('illa). A later mujtahid may legitimately differ if he identifies a different 'illa, even if the first mujtahid had already established a ruling. This acknowledges that on analogical matters, valid ikhtilaf can arise across time because the 'illa is subject to ijtihad.
This nuance shows that the contemporaneity condition applies primarily to non-qiyasi issues rooted in transmitted evidence.
5
Reflect on the divine favor aspect
The author concludes that the confinement of mainstream schools to four is not a matter of logical necessity but a divine favor. This humility reminds us that ultimate legal authority rests with Allah.
This is a recognition of the historical development of madhahib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإجماع
بيان مراتب أهل الإجماع
أن يكون مذهب الشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل حلها، باطلا، حين اختلف أبو حنيفة رحمه الله مع مالك رحمه الله في زمان واحد، وإن أريد بالاختلاف أعم من أن يكون في زمان واحد أم لا فكيف لا يعتبر اختلافنا كما اعتبر اختلاف الشافعي وأحمد ابن حنبل رحمهما الله؟ والجواب عنه صعب، وقد بالغت في تحقيقه في التفسير الأحمدي، وبذلت جهدي وطاقتي فيه، ولم يسبقني إلى مثله أحد، فطالعه إن شئت.
مذهب الشافعي له إلخ: إذ لا مشافهة للشافعي رحمه الله وأحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله لأبي حنيفة رحمه الله. (القمر) باطلا لأنهما كانا بعد أبي حنيفة ومالك رحمهما الله. (المحشي)
في تحقيقه إلخ حتى أورد الجواب بقوله: الاختلاف المعتبر هو الذي في زمان واحد، والشافعي رحمه الله وغيره إذا قالوا قولاً إنما يقولون إذا جرى رأي أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله مع أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، أو كان اختلاف بين الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم، فأخذ أبو حنيفة بقول صحابي، ومالك والشافعي أخذ بقول صحابي آخر، والأغلب أن شيئًا من المسائل لا يكون فيه أربع أقوال للأئمة الأربعة، بل يكون فيه قولان أو ثلاث، وبعض من الأئمة يتبعون البعض، ولا يلزم أن يكون لكل من الأئمة الأربعة قول في كل، وهكذا الحال في أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله وغيرهما، ولعل هذا أي اتحاد الزمان في غير المسائل القياسية، وأما المسائل القياسية فالمدار فيها على العلة، فمهما وجدها المجتهد مخالفًا للأول أو موافقًا له يعمل به، والإنصاف أن انحصار المذاهب في الأربع واتباعهم فضل إلهي، وقبولية من عند الله تعالى لا مجال فيه للتوجيهات والأدلة. (القمر)
Chapter of Consensus. Explanation of the ranks of the people of consensus. That the school of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal is invalid [as a basis to reject consensus] because Abu Hanifa disagreed with Malik in one time period. If disagreement is meant generally, whether in one time or not, then why is our disagreement not considered just as the disagreement of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal is considered? The answer to this is difficult, and I have gone to great lengths in investigating it in al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi, and I exerted my effort and capacity in it, and no one preceded me in the like of it, so look into it if you wish. 'The school of al-Shafi'i, etc.' — because al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal did not directly address Abu Hanifa. (al-Qamar) 'Invalid' because they came after Abu Hanifa and Malik. (al-Muhashi) 'In investigating it etc.' until he presented the answer saying: 'The disagreement that is considered is that which occurs in one time period. Al-Shafi'i and others, when they say an opinion, they only say it if the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concurred with Abu Hanifa, or there was a disagreement among the Companions, so Abu Hanifa took the statement of one Companion, and Malik and al-Shafi'i took the statement of another Companion. Most often, a particular issue does not have four opinions from the four imams; rather it has two or three, and some imams follow others. It is not necessary that each of the four imams has an opinion on every issue. The same is the case with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and others. And perhaps this [i.e., unity of time] applies to non-qiyasi issues. As for qiyasi issues, the criterion is the effective cause ('illa); so whenever a mujtahid finds it, whether differing from the first or agreeing with him, he acts upon it. The fair view is that the restriction of schools to four and their following is a divine favor, something accepted from Allah Almighty, with no room for rationalizations and evidence.' (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage addresses the concept of ijma' (consensus) and which differences of opinion among scholars are considered valid when determining whether consensus exists. The author begins by stating that the schools of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal cannot be used to reject a consensus claimed by earlier scholars like Abu Hanifa and Malik, because these later imams were not contemporaries of Abu Hanifa. The key point is that for a difference of opinion to prevent consensus, the opposing scholars must have been alive at the same time. This is why the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Malik is relevant, but the later opinions of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad are not, since they did not directly engage with Abu Hanifa. The author acknowledges the difficulty and refers to his own work for a full treatment. The marginal notes clarify that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad came after Abu Hanifa and Malik, so their opinions are not contemporaneous. The commentator (al-Qamar) further elaborates that the only valid differences are those among contemporaries, or those that trace back to disagreements among the Companions which the later imams adopted. Often, not all four imams have distinct opinions on every issue; some follow others. This is also true for the students of Abu Hanifa like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The passage then distinguishes between issues based on independent reasoning (qiyasi) and those based on transmitted evidence. For qiyasi issues, the ruling depends on the effective cause ('illa), so a later mujtahid may differ if he finds a different cause. Finally, the author asserts that the confinement of the mainstream schools to four is a matter of divine grace, not something that can be logically justified.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids of the Muslim community on a legal ruling after the death of the Prophet.
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilaf): Disagreement among scholars on a legal ruling. In this context, only contemporaneous ikhtilaf is relevant for ijma'.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause in qiyas (analogical reasoning). For qiyasi issues, the ruling depends on the 'illa as determined by the mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the school of al-Shafi'i not considered a valid difference that can prevent consensus claimed by Abu Hanifa and Malik?
A) Because al-Shafi'i agreed with Abu Hanifa on all issues.
B) Because al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal were not contemporaries of Abu Hanifa and Malik.
C) Because al-Shafi'i based his rulings on the Quran alone.
D) Because the consensus of Abu Hanifa and Malik was already recorded.

Key Takeaway
Only differences among contemporaries prevent consensus; later divergences either follow earlier views or are based on different 'illa, and the restriction to four schools is a divine favor.
